SRI LANKA
MAHAWELI GANGA DEVELOPMENT PROJECT IV
BACKGROUND
1.1     The Mahaweli Ganga Development Program has been the centerpiece of the
Government of Sri Lanka's (GOSL) public investment effort since the current
administration came to power. In late 1977, GOSL decided to accelerate implemen-
tation of the Progra'a to reduce food imports, unemployment, and energy deficien-
cies and to provide settlement opportunities. An exceptional amount of financial
support was generated from international donors for accelerated program develop-
ment. The proposed project would be the fourth in a series of irrigated agricul-
tural projects to be assisted by the Bank Group under the Mahaweli Program: the
first project has been completed; the second is expected to be completed by
mid-1984; and the third, appraised in 1980 and started in 1982, is sti:ll in early
implementation stages. In addition, the Bank has played an active role in advis-
ing on the composition of the Mahaweli Program and in coordinating external assis-
tance for its financing.
1.2     GOSL has attached high priority for System B development for two main
reasons: (a) to utilize water resources from the recently completed Maduru Oya
Reservoir and (b) to serve as irrigated resettlement areas for persons from
upstream reservoir basins where dams are currently being constructed as well as
from overpopulated, economically depressed areas elsewhere in the country. In
early 1983, GOSL formally requested Bank participation in financing development of
either the System B Left or Right Dank downstream projects. CIDA and the Saudi
Fund for Development have indicated their intent to co-finance development of the
Right Bank (RB). Because of USAID's expected expanded role in financing Left Bank
(LB) development with co-financing from Australia, the Saudi Fund for Development,
and the EEC, and the ongoing implementation of that work, the Bank has opted to
participate in RB development and thereby to remove its outstanding financing gap.
1.3     In 1980, a feasibility report for System B was prepared by Acres Interna-
tional, Ltd., with financing and supervision f¢rom GOSL. A revised report for the
RB project, including updated technical and cost data, was prepared by Acres in
1982 with CIDA financing. Detailed designs of the RB main and branch canals were
carried out in late 1981 and 1982 by the Berger/IECO consulting consortium, with
USAID financing, as part of the consortium's LB irrigation system design studies.
These reports formed the basis for appraisal by a joint Bank/CIDA mission which
visited Sri Lanka in June-July 1983. The appraisal mission comprised S. Baker, P.
Tamboli, W. Nickel, S. Lysy (IDA) and J.W. Robins (consultant) and V. Carvell
(CIDA). A detailed land classification survey was carried out by the Mahaweli
Authority of Sri Lanka (MASL) in support of the appraisal with the assistance of a
soil specialist and a drainage engineer (consultants).
I. SECTORAL BACKGROUND
General
1.01    Sri Lanka is endowed with favorable resources and diverse agro-climatic
conditions that permit wide-ranging agricultural pursuits. Sri Lanka comprises an
area of 65,600 sq km with a total population estimated at 15.2 M (1982) growing at
about 1.7% p.a. GDP per capita is about US$320. The population is highly
literate and has achieved considerable success in curbing population growth.



-2-
About 75% of the population lives in rural areas and is directly or indlirectly
dependent on agriculttLre.  The climate is characterized by nearly constant tem-
peratures and large variations in rainfall. The island can be divided into the
distinct wet anld intermediate zones (average annual ra-inifall over 1,900i mm) and
dry zone (average annual rainifall below 1,900 mnm).  The combined wet an' inter-
mediaLe zone occurs in the southwest quadrant and covers about 30% of Srl. Lanka's
land aren.  This zone supports more than 75% of the population and accoun,s for
about 45% of cultivated area. 1/
1.02    Until the late 1970s, economic growth in Sri Lanka was slow.  Real per
capita GDP grew at 2.1% p.a. in the 1960s and declined to 1.3% p.a. during tile
1970-76 period.  The slow pace of economic growth in the 1970s was mainly due to a
combinf2tion of inadequate investment, ineffective and inappropriate goveriimeat
policies tosward growth and investment, adverse weather, and a sharp rise in "lhe
cost of imported food and fuel.  This disappointing economic performance resulted
in severe unemployment.  In late 1977, the newly elected administration initiated
a "liberalization" strategy to achieve higher growth rates of: (a) production and
employment, (b) savings and investment, and (c) exports.  Major changes included
exchange rate unification and de2preciation, import trade liberalization, interest
rate increases to encourage savings, full-cost pricing for most imported com-
modities, and a free trade zone to attract export-oriented foreign private invest-
ment, The economy reacted vigorously to these changes. Sri Lanka'B development
efforts since 1977 have yielded an annual growth rate in GDP in excess of 6%. The
primary sectors of significant growth have been paddy production, services, and
construction, These benefited from changes in relative prices, removal of import
quotas, and rapidly expanded investment.  Furthermore, employment growth has been
substantial in both the public and private sectors. Unemployment has been reduced
further by out-migration in response to job opportunities abroad. As a result,
the unemployment rate fell from 24% of the labor force in 1975, to 14.8% in 1978
and 13.1% in 1982. 2/ Fixed investment likewise has risen from 14-15% of GDP in
the early 1970s to 30% in recent years.
riculture Sector
1.03    Agriculture plays a major role in Sri Lanka's economy, accounting
for abour 25% of GDP, over 50% of the labor force, and about 60% of export
earvings. About 95% of the rural population is involved directly in
agriculture-related activities.  In addition, much of the activity in the manufac-
turing, transport and services sectors relates to either agricultural input supply
or the processing agricultural produce.  Sri Lanka's climate is suitable for
year-round cultivation, and variations in precipitation, topography, temperature,
and soils enable production of a wide range of crops. Out of about 2.24 M ha
under permanent cultivation (34% of land area), paddy accoonts for 0.65 M ha; tree
crops (coconut, tea, and rubber) 0.93 M ha; and other perennial crops 0.04 4 ha.
The remaining 0.62 M ha comprises mixed rainfed farming, mostly as small gardens
around homesteads. An additional 1.0 M ha are under shifting slash and burn
cultivation (chena).
1/ Department of Census and Statistics, Census of Agriculture - 1973, December
1977.
2/  Based on consumer finance of households sample surveys by the Central tank.



-3-
1.04    GOSL's current policy emphasizes development and settlenietit of the dry
zone thirough the Accelerated Mahaweli Ganga Development Program (AbIDP) while
complemnenting these long-gestation investments wit:h lower-cost, quick-yielding
projects in areas receiving no significant berefits from tlhe Malhaweli Program.
Investment and incentive policy chianges introduced for the agricultur&l sector by
GOSL in the late 1970s were a response to disappointing growth in the early and
mid-1970s.  Average agricultural growth, 3.4% p.a. during the 1960s, had declined
to only 1.9% p.a. over the 1970-77 period. Agricultural performance over tile
1978-82 period improved substantially with growth averaging 4.0% p.a. This over-
all result was buoyed by an impressive inicrease in paddy production averaging 5.2
% p.a., and was depressed by poorer growth in the tree crops sector averaging only
1.6% p.a.  As a consequence, imports of rice have declined dramatically.  Climat.c
vagaries, however, have been particularly pronounced in the last three yeqr: and
have had marked impact on Sri Lankan agriculture.  A marginal decline in paddy
production in 1982 of 3.3% was attributable to an 11% drop in the 1981-82 maha
crop due to a severe drought, mitigated by a 1982 yala crop 12% better than the
previous year. 1/ The 1982-83 maha crop (1.79 M tons), in contrast, was a
record--31% above the previous year's depressed production. Drought conditions
during the 1983 yala and maha 1983-84 seasons were very serious, leaving normally
sown areas either uncultivated or damaged.
Irrigation Subsector
1.05    Annual mean rainfall in Sri Lanka's wet and intermediate agro-climatic
zones ranges from about 2,200 to 3,900 mm and is fairly well distributed
throughout the year. Paddy can be grown in these zones under rainfed conditions,
not only in the maha season but, in some areas, in the yala as well. Irrigation
development in the wet zone has been limited primarily to run-of-the-river diver-
sion. In contrast, annual rainfall in the dry zone, averaging only about 1,300
mm, is concentrated in the maha season and displays considerable year-to-year
variability; irrigation, therefore, is essential for cultivation in the yala
season, and some supplemental irrigation is often necessary for the maha crop.
The dry zone, containing about 285,000 ha of irrigated land, accounts for about
78% of the total irrigated area. Traditional irrigation schemes are based on
storage tanks designed to provide supplemental irrigation to the maha crop, with
any residual water used for 'Limited dry season cropping. There are a total of
about 180 such major schemes in Sri Lanka with a combined storage capacity in
excess of 2.5 M n:3 (irrigating a total of nearly 162,000 ha) and a vast number of
smaller schemes. Traditionally, maintenance of these schemes has been the respon-
sibility of farmers, with only major headworks and main canals under government
purview. For most schemes, water is allowed to flow continuously, running from
field to field with only limited development of tertiary channels. Not only have
most of these irrigation systems deteriorated seriously but initial designs were
outmoded and provided only minimal control. With modernization, proper operation,
improved water management practices, and introduction of appropriate cropping
patterns (in particular to optimize rainfall use and to diversify into non-paddy
crops on more permeable soils), substantial increases in cropping intensities and
irrigable areas could be attained with existing water supplies. Under the ongoing
1/ Maha is the northeast monsoon season (October-February); yala is the southwest
monsoon season (April-September).



-4..
Tank Irrigation Modernization and Mahaweli II and III Projects (Cr. 666-CE, Cr.
701-CE, and Cr. 1166-CE), Rural Development Projects I, II and III (Cr. 891-CE,
Cr. 1079-CE, and Cr. 1363-CE) and Village Irrigation Rehabilitation Project
(Cr. 1160-CE), IDA is providing support for improved water management and
prototypes for future development of other dry zone tanks. For the longer-term;,
GOSL is emphasizing expansion of irrigated agriculture in the dry zone through
development of Sri Lanka's major river basins, principally the Mahaweli Ganga
Basin.
Mahaweli Ganga Development Program 1/
1.06    The basic objectives underlying the Mahaweli Ganga Development Program
stem from a need to overcome three major problems facing Sri Lanka: (a) unemploy-
ment; (b) excessive imports of foodgrain and other agricultural products; and (c)
a shortage of power for industrial development and rural electrification. Under
the Program, about 225,000 farm families would be settled on newly developed land,
and over a million people would be employed in agricultural and allied pursuits.
Agricultural output would redi'ce the need to import rtce and other food items for
which the foreign exchange 1rill averaged about US$270 M in 1980-82, or about 25%
of export earnings.
1.07    The Program is based on a Master Plan prepared jointly by a UNDP/FAO team
and Sri Lankan engineers in 1965-68. The Plan, which envisaged development of
about 365,000 ha of irrigable land in the dry zone as well as about 500 MW of
hydropower, was divided into three phases, each including several projects for
stepwise implementation over a 30-year period. Implementation of the Plan began
in 1970. Late in 1977, however, GOSL realized thit at the current rates of con-
struction and population growth, the Program would not have much near-term impact
on the unemployment problem and serious food and power shortages, and that it
should be accelerated. An Implementation Strategy Study was carried out with
financing from the Netherlands to: (a) review and re-evaluate the UNDP/FAO Master
Plan and each of its separate projects, (b) examine the implications of various
alternative phasings of the feasible projects, and (c) recommend a specific action
plan taking into account technical, financial, economic, institutional, and man-
power constraints. While the Study was underway, GOSL selected five major
projects comprising five dams and hydropower plants with a total potential power
capacity of 500-600 MW and downstream development of about 112,000 ha to be
included under its Accelerated Mahaweli Development Program (AMDP). Donors were
approached for support for different components of the Program and responded
positively. Meanwhile, the Study, completed in 1979, concluded that (a) the base
costs of the overall Program had been greatly underestimated; (b) two of the five
projects (Randenigala and Moragahakanda) were not required for irrigation in the
AMDP as the remaining Kotmale, Victoria and the Maduru Oya Dams would provide
sufficient regulation to irrigate the entire AMDP area; and (c) based on power
demand studies, substantial additions to existing capacity were needed.
1/ The Mahaweli Ganga Development Program or Scheme, as shown in the UNDP/FAO
Mahaweli Ganga Master Plan of 1968, includes about 365,000 ha of net irrigable
land situated in the Mahaweli Ganga and Maduru Oya Basins (192,000 ha) and the
North-Central part of Sri Lanka (17S,000 ha). The Accelerated Mahaweli
Development Program encompasses about one-third of the Scheme's net irrigable
area, representing Systems A, B, C, D-1, D-2, E, F, and G.



-5-
1.08    GCSL accepted most of these concluvlons and revised the AMDP accordingly,
with the exception of including the Randenigala Project since it was justified on
power alone and it was considered badly needed by about 1989. Initial work in
AMDP (1980-85) was confined to the four headworks projects having a total
installed capacity of about 500 MW (Kotmale, financed partly by Swedish import
support funds; Victoria, by an initial 100 M UK grant; Maduru Oya, by Canadian
loans and grants amounting to C$85.9 M; and Randenigala, by a D)M 400 M loan from
the Federal Republic or Germany).  Downstream development was to be limited to
about 60,000 ha in Systems B and C, completion of ongoing development works in
System H, and coastruction of the M4inipe Anicut, the Right Bank Transbasin Canal,
and the Ulhitya and Ratkinda Reservoirs. The US is currently providing assistance
for downstream development of the Maduru Oya Project System B LB (US$90 M); it
also financed an in-depth study of the environmental impact of AMDP and is financ-
ing a Mahaweli Environmental Project (US$5.0 M) to implement the Study's recommen-
dations. Canada and the UK financed feasibility studies of Systems B and C,
respectively. The EEC is supporting development of Zone 2 in System C; and IDA,
Japan, and the Kuwait Fund are assisting in Zones 3-6 of System C. The Asian
Development Bank is providing assistance to plan and construct roads in the AMDP
area. Under a technical assistance credit, IDA ard the EEC financed consultancy
services to review designs and tender documents for the Transbasin Canal and for
detailed designs and engineering of irrigation infrastructure in System C. The
proposed project in System B RB would mark the Bank's second area development
effort in the AMDP area. The Bank also financed an earlier Mahaweli project to
divert Mahaweli Ganga water to System H and adjoining areas (ptra 1.10).
1.09    The Mahaweli Program and Sri Lanka's Public Investment Program.  The
overall costs of the Program have risen substantially during the past yeats due to
rapid domestic and international inflation and to increased costs associated with
more difficult site conditions for the Kotmale project (a major storage dam under
the AMDP) than originally anticipated. Its share of total public capital expendi-
tures rose from about 7% in 1977 to about 35% over 1980-82, reaching a high of 46%
in 1982. As a consequence of its relative and absolute magnitudes, AMDP has (a)
contributed substantially to large unfinanced budget deficits, (b) led to heavy
dependence on external financing, including sizable commercial borrowings, and (c)
helped create a serious imbalance between current and capital spending. The
macro-economic situation, including the need to reduce public investment and
reorient public spending generaliy, is the topic of ongoing dialogue between GOSL,
the Bank and the IMF. GOSL has responded to macro-management difficulties by
making various adjustments and cuts in the capital budget, including adjustments
to AMDP (e.g., reducing the height of Kotmale Dam and deferring some downstream
development). In addition, GOSL imposed a moratorium during 1983-84 on new
projects not already in its investment program. Nevertheless, the extent to which
Mahaweli can contribute to GOSL's overall adjustment in its public investment
program are limited by earlier commitments, expenditures on which are expected
to peak over 1982-84. Thereafter, declines are projected, and Mahaweli's share is
currently expected to fall to 23% of total public investment by 1985-87. The
proposed project for System B RB development would basically be implemented after
the current peak. It is also intended to complement major headwork investments
(sunk costs) already made in the Maduru Oya Reservoir and RB Transbasin Canal and
to utilize other external assistance.

Bank Group's Rolt in Mahaweli Ganga Development
1.10    Withi Bank Group assistance, initial construction under the Pr,gram bega
in 1970 under the Mahaweli Ganga Development Project I (Cr. 653-CE) with divers±  i
headworks at Polgolla on the Mahaweli Ganga and at Bow;tenna on the Amban Ganga,
including a 40 MW hydropower plant at Polgolla. This first project was completed
in 1978 and provided an improved water supply to about 52,650 ha of existi.ig
irrigated land and a full supply for about 29,000 ha of unirrigated land in System
H to be developed under future projects. The project was well designed, fulfilled
its objectives, and received full support from government agencies. In a
Bank-sponsored audit of the completed project, it was also noted that farmers'
ability to quickly adopt double-cropping was one of the principal reasons for its
success.  Under Mahaweli II (Cr. 701-CE), with co-financing from IDA, Canada, the
Netherlands, UK, US, and EEC, construction work is nearing completion on irriga-
tion and social infrastructure for about 60% of this 29,000 ha of newly irrigated
area. 1/ The Project, including settlement assistance and agricultural extension
and support services, is e-cpected to be completed by mid-1984, or about one year
behind schedule. Under Mahaweli III (Cr. 1166-CE), with co-financing from IDA,
Japan and the Kuwait Fund, work on downstream development in System C was started
in 1980 with award of contracts for construction of the Minipe RB Transbasin
Canal. The construction of irrigation infrastructure works began in 1982. The
Project will provide a full irrigation supply for about 24,100 ha of newly
irrigated land and a supplemental supply for about 3,600 ha in System C. The
Project includes irrigation and social infrastructure, settlement assistance, and
agricultural development assistance. Concentrated principally in Zones 3-6 of
System C, the Project is complemented by similar concomitant investments in Zone 2
financed by EEC. Under a technical assistance credit (Cr. 979-CE), IDA, with EEC
co-financing, is providing technical assistance for a study to determine optimal
use of surplus water from the Mahaweli Ganga. In addition to financing these
earlier projects, the Bank has also assisted GOSL with AMDP principally throlgh
external assistance coordination for project preparation and implementation.
Implementation Progress of Mahaweli Ganga DevE lpment Projects II and III
1.11    Implementation of Mahaweli II is resulting in a well-developed agricul-
tural area with promise for sustained, increased agricultural production. Quality
social infrastructure has been provided. However, some additional work is
required to improve water management and rectify water measurement and control
structures. Further, as the Project has become operational, additional canal
lining requirements have been identified to reduce excessive canal seepage losses
and concomitant drainage problems. Although necessary canal lining was foreseen
at appraisal and included under the Project, only limited sections of the canal
have been lined thus far. Remaining construction of structures and canal lining
are matched to local funds available, which are currently deemed insufficient to
finance all the work needed. It may therefore be possible, in view of time and
resource constraints, to finish no more than 60% of the lining works foreseen.
The Credit was extended one year. System H has become an important contributor to
national rice production, and output of other field crops is steadily increasing.
Maha 1982-83 paddy yields were reported to average 5.4 tons/ha against a national
1/ GOSL independently carried out development of the other 40% prior to donor
involvement in Mahaweli II in order to develop this area quickly.

average of 3.5 tons/ha, suggesting that the emphasis of the extension program on
timely cultivation, fertilizer application and weed control is appropriate. Land
acquisition payments and disbursements from the Credit are behind schedule. Cost

begun.
1.12    Implemeintation of Mahaweli III is still in early stages, although con-

II. PROJECT AREA
General
2.01    System B, the downstream command area of the recently compleced Madura Oya
Dam, consists of a net irrigable area of about 39,400 ha out of a gross area of
136,000 ha. It includes about one third of the lands to be irrigated under AMDP.
This irrigable area is located on both the right and left banks of the Maduru Oya
River downstream from Maduru Oya Reservoir, lying east of the Mahaweli Ganga in an
adjacent basin within Polonnaruwa, Trincomalee, and Batticaloa Districts. The
water supply for Systems B and C has been developed tarough construction of the
Victoria Dam and Reservoir, Minipe Anicut Right Bank rransbasin Canal, and the
Ulhitiya-Ratkinda Reservoirs. Irrigation facilities for the System B LB area
(about 25,400 net irrigable ha) are currently being developed with assistance from
USAID, the Saudi Fund and Australia.
2.02    The project area is located on the RB of the Maduru Oya and comprises a
gross area of 58,000 ha with an estimated net irrigable area of 14,000 ha (Map No.
IBRD 17493). The RB area is located entirely in Batticaloa District and covers
specifically Zones 6-8 of System B. About 80% of the RB is currently covered by
scrub evergreen forest, 12% under wetland paddy cultivation, 5% under chena, and
3% by traditional settlements and tanks. By 1979 much of the forested area was
subjected to unsanctioned encroachment, although to a lesser extent than in other
Mahaweli areas such as System C. Traces and roads have since been cut to enable
topographic and soil surveys and timber extraction. Virtually all marketable
timber has been removed in preparation for settlement and agricultural develop-
ment.
2.03    The population of the total System B area was estimated in 1979 at 26,000.
However, the RB area has a population of less than 7,000. Most of the RB popula-
tion, which is en,aged in agriculture, is concentrated in about six villages.
Cultivated land, now used principally for paddy, totals about 5,600 gross
ha--2,000 ha under the Punanai river diversion scheme, 600 ha uncler minor tanks,



-8-
and 3,000 ha of rainfed floodplains. The larger vilLages and rmost of the popula-
tion are located near the relatively extensive paddy areas in Zones 7-8. A few
families mnaintain cattle for meat and/or milk, production.  About 2,000 ha of chena
are cultivated each year, and the pressure on suitable lands has resulced in
shortening the shifting cultivation cycle and encroachment on lands desigrated as
forest reserves. Most families outside the areas served by diversion or tank
irrigation are eligible for the National Food Stamp Scheme (threshold income
eligibility level of Rs 3,600 p.a. per family).
2.04    Infrastructure development is rather rudimentary in the RB area.  An
unsurfaced road, traversing the western end of Zone 7 and the Length of Zone 8
(the Wacaanunne-Kirane Road), is generally passable year-round.  The remaining road
network is limited to cart tracks.  A few small villages in Zones 7-8 provide sore
rudimentary services, but residents generally have to travel outside the area for
satisfactory communications, health, education, and technical services. Agricul-
tural supporting services within the RB area are negligible and are generally
confined to extension activities around area tanks. Services are more adequate at
Welikanda, Punanai town, and Vakaneri, all located on the LB north of and adjacent
to Zone 7, and around Valachchenai, east of Zone 8.  The main Colombo-Batticoloa
rail :,ine passes just north of the RB area, making stops at Welikanda and Punanai.
Welikanda currently serves as the principal construction headquarters for System B
development.
Clirmate
2.05    The warm tropical climate in the RB area is favorable for year-round crop
production. Temperatures average about 28oC, varying from occasIonal lows of 170C
in January-February to highs near 380C during Jv..e-September.  Mean annual rain-
fall is about 1,800 mm with about 65% occurring during the NE monsoon period from    J
nid-October to mid-February (maha), and the balance during the reMainder of the
year (yala). Rainfall unpredictability in the naha makes irrigation necessary to
ensture high and stable yields. Crop cultivation during the yala is almost
entirely dependent on irrigation.
Topography and Drainage
2.06    Elevation and surface configuration of RB lands is generally controlled by
underlying bedrock. The depth-to-bedrock generaLly exceeds six meters in lowland
areas. However, this barrier is relatively shallow beneath the higher lands where
soils and unconsolidated substrata are typically 2-4 in deep.  Rock outcrops are
common, especially on upper slopes and ridge crests.  Floodplains, comprising the
greater part of the valley floor, have general sLopes of less than 0.5% with
smooth to gently undulating microrelief.  Higher lands, consisting of a dissected
erosional surface that comprises about 75% of the gross area, have the most
pronounced relief.  Gradients on side slopes of the broader ridges are generally
in the range of 1-3%. Some side slopes of narrow ridges have general gradient
exceeding 6%. Floodplains are poorly to very poorly drained.  In most areas the
watertable rises :o surface level in the maha season, and the lowest sections were
subject to flood ag in the normal maha season prior to Maduru Oya Reservoir con-
struction.  Howeever, flooding will be much less extensive and severe with
anticipated reservoir operations. Scattered tracts of moderateLy well drained
lands are found in upland areas having permeable soils with relatively deep bar-
rier zones. However, the greater portion of uplands is poorly drained because of
relatively shallow depth to effective barrier. The watertable rises close to the



- 9-
surface in thiese areas during normal maha seasons under currently nonirrigated
conditions.
Soils and Land Suitability for Irrigation Development
2.07    Soil salinity, exchangeable sodium, or iron toxicity would not be problems
for RB irrigation development considering (a) area rainfall and irrigation water
quality, (b) adequacy of drainage to meet leaching requirements, and (c) favorable
soil chemical characteristics. Lands vary considerably in other important soil
characteristics that, together with topography and drainage, determine their
suitability for irrigation and appropriate cropping patterns.
2.08    Floodpla:n lands have a flat regular topography, relatively high-fertility
soils, and fairly impermeable soils ideal for paddy production. About two thirds
of the higher lands above the floodplains are considered nonirrigable because of
steep topography, coarse textured soils with low cation-exchange capacity, and/or
shallow soil depth. Of those higher lands classified as irrigable, the majority
have been designated for paddy production because their shallow depth-to-barrier
would otherwise require extensive drainage to make non-paddy cropping technically
feasible.  The rtumaining scattered tracts of irrigable lands having deep, perme-
able, fertile soils would t'n suitable for upland crop production.  Irrigable land
areas designated for paddy and upland crops are summarized in Annex 1, Table 1.
Rural Credit
2.09    Institutional rural credit in Sri Lanka is principally disbursed by two
nationalized commercial banks, the People's Bank (PB) and Bank of Ceylon (BC). PB
operates through its banking divisions, the Cooperative Rural Banks.  BC provides
credit services through its main branches, with representation maintained at
Agrarian Service Centers (ASC). National rural credit policy is established by a
Rural Credit Advisory Board (RCAB) chaired by the Governor of the Central Bank
(CB).  CB's Department of Rural Credit functions as the Board's secretariat and
executive. Within Mahaweli Program areas, BC and PB always operate in separate
jurisdictions, at the discretion of the Mahaweli Economic Agency (MEA), so that
any farner will have access to only one banking institution. At the present time,
there are no banking facilities in the System B RB area.
2.10    The total amount of institutional rural credit disbursed nationwide
declined in nominal terns from Rs 156.8 M (1981) to Rs 153.9 M (1982), or by 1.8%.
This decline was primarily the result of a drop in lending under the Comprehensiva
Rural Credit Scheme (CRCS) by 13. During the same period, crop cultivation loans
declined from Rs 85.3 M to Rs 75.2 M. High default rates continue to be an
unsatisfactory feature of rural lending. From 1967 to end-1982, initially under
the New Agricultural Credit Scheme and subsequently under the CRCS, only about 48%
of amounts disbursed have been recovered. 1/ In 1982, only Rs 86.2 M of the Rs
150.4 M disbursed during the 1981-82 cultivation year was recovered, resulting in
a deterioration in the recovery rates for both paddy and minor food crops compared
to the previous year. The maha 1981-82 drought accounted for part of this
deterioration. As a result of this drought and subsequent defaults, the National
1/  From 1967 to end-1982, Rs 1,973 K was issued for crop cultivation but only Rs
965 M was recovered.



-10-
Agricultural Credit Fund (NACF) was established by the RCAB to provide insurance
for crop failures due to natural calamities. Such insurance thereafter was
required for crop loans; however, the Crop Insurance Scheme has now bean suspended
as a result of the impact on its operatiors of subsequent droughts.
2.11    With a view to improving recoveries, the RCAB in 1981 started a reschedul-
ing effort for CRCS cultivation. loans in default.  Under the schene, defaulters
can be eligible for new loans, initially paying 10% of the overdue loan aLong withi
interest at 9.0% p.a. up to June 30, 1981. Thereafter, at; each harvest, a fonrer
defaulter is expected to repay the balance over a five year period. Nqo literest
was charged after June 30, 1981.  If farmers pay the first seven installrnents
regulariy, the scheme provides a waiver for the last three installments.  The
scheme was adopted by BC in 1982 and then by PB in 1983.  By ernd-1982 the totaL
amount of loans coDsolidated indicated that farmers' response to the scheme was
greater than expected. This national rescheduling effort shouLd estabLish. a
healthier farmer-bank relationship as a basis for the future credit expansion.
2.12    Cultivation loans from banks for paddy and subsidiary food crops are
generally provided for 240 days at 9.0% interest p.a., witb a penalty of 5%
charged on overdues.  Such loans are provided partly in kind (with direct payment
to input suppliers) and partly in cash. Long-term credit is aLso avaiLabLe to the
farmers for purchasing equipment.  In addition to this institutional credit, there
is evidence that a substantial share of rural credit comes fromn non-institurional
sources.  Data from System H indicate tnat only 22.5% of settlers received loans
from institutional sources between yala 1980 and mnaha 1982-83. A large proportion
of the remainder is likely to have taken credit from non-institutional lenders.
This aspect of rural credit is currently under study by the Mahaweli Authority of
Sri Lanka (MASL) and banking agencies.
III. THE PROJECT
General
3.01    The proposed project would be che fourth irrigated agricultural develop-
ment project assisted by the Bank Group under the hahaweli Ganga Development
Program. The project would provide facilities for new irrigation and set:tlement
on about 14,000 ha (net) lying on the Right Bank of the Maduru Oya and for
enhanced irrigation to about 1,800 ha (net) of existing cultivated area,
non-irrigated conservation forests, fuelwood and cashew plantations, and axtisanal
fisheries development. It would also include: management facilities, staff
quarters and vehicles; social infrastructure and settlement assistance; rechnical
assistance; monitoring, training, and environmental enhanceaent programs; and
equipment and vehicles for construc2tion, operation, training and crop cuLtivation
programs.
Principal Project Features
3.02    Works, facilities and programs to be implemented would inaclude:
(a)  construction of NDK Dam and Feeder Canal (7.1 kn);
(b)  construction of Right Bank Main and Branch Canals;



-1 1-
(c)  construction of an irrigation distribution and drainage system to serve
14,060 ha (net) of newly irrigated area and to enhance the irrigation
potential of 1,800 ha (net) within the existing Punanai settlement scheme;
(d)  construction of main and secondary roads;
(e) clearing of 20,000 ha for agricultural and plantation developmen. and
construction of on-farm facilities for 14,000 ha (net);
(f)  construction of social infrastructure and facilities for new settlement
communities;
(g) construction of operation and management offices and quarters;
(h) settler assistance of temporary shelter, house grants, tools, and seeds;
(i) establishment of cashew and fuelwood plantations;
(j) provision of agricultural extension and supporting services and research
facilities;
(k) a monitoring and evaluation program;
(1) an environmental enhancement and monitoring program;
(m) provision of technical assistance and training;
(n) equipment and vehicles for construction supervision and Operation and
Maintenance (C&M);
(o) annual stocking of area waterbodies for artisanal fisheries development.
Description of Project Works, Facilities, and Programs
3.03    NDK Dam and Reservoir (Annex 2, Table 5).  A small regulatory reservoir
would be provided on the RB Main Canal downstream of the outlet from Maduru Oya
Reservoir. The reservoir formed by NDK Dam would have an active capacity of 15.6
M m3 and would partially regulate natural stream inflows plus releases for the RB
from Maduru Oya Dam. This reservoir would also act as afterbay storage for
releases from power facilities at the Maduru oya Dam should they eventually be
developed.  The NDK Dam would be 2,700 m long (including two low dikes) and 18 mi
high above streambed with a crest at 78 X elevation.  The spillway, with a crest
width of 275 m at 74.5 m elevation, would have maximum discharge capacity of 1,900
m3/sec.
3.04    Main and Branch Canals (Annex 2, Table 6).  The Main Canal from Maduru Oya
Reservoir to NDK Dam would have a capacity of 100 m3/sec; from NDK Dam the
capacity would be 28 m3/sec. The canal would be about 35 km long and would be
concrete-lined throughout. Nine branch canals, totalling 55 km in length with
capacities varying from 1-10 m3/sec, would also be eoncrete-lined. Eight small
reservoirs, formed by level canal crossings and with capacities of from 0.25 - 1.5
Km3, would be located along the canal alignment.



-12-
3.05    Irrigation Distribution and Drainage Facilities (Annex 2, Table 7).  About
280 km of distributary canals and 765 km of field canals would be constructed to
distribute irrigation water from main and branch canals to the estimated 14,000 ha
of net irrigable area in Zones 6-8, All distributary canals serving upland areas
(28 km) and about 10% (25 km) of the canals serving lowlands would be con-
crete-lined. A farm turnout would be provided to serve each one-ha farm plot in
lowland areas and one turnout would be provided for every two ha in upland areas.
About 680 km of field drains, 200 km of secondary drains, and 35 km of main drains
would be constructed for protection against excessive water level buildup from
rains and to control the subsurface watertable in upland areas.
3.06    Roads (Annex 2, Table 8).  The road network constructed under the project
would include about 75 km of 11 m-wide primary trunk roads, 230 km of 7-m wide
market and access roads (including 30 km of O&M roads upgraded to market road
standards), and 140 km of 4 mn-wide village and hamlet roads. Trunk and market
roads would be asphalt-surfaced and village and hamlet roads would be laterite- or
gravel-surfaced.
3.07    Land Clearing and On-farm Development (Annex 2, Table 9).  A total of
about 15,000 ha would require clearing to provide a net irrigable area of 14,000
ha of farm land, and 5,000 ha would be cleared for the cashew, fuelwood and con-
servation forest plantations. Clearing of homesteads would not be included under
the project so that settlerE could utilize vegetation for fuel and shade. Rough
leveling, land shaping and bund layouts would be carried out under the project for
the 14,000 ha area. Construction of bunds, on-farm ditches and drains, and final
farm leveling or grading would be done by settlers on a labor contract basis.
3.08    Social Infrastructure and Community Development Facilities (Annex 2, Table
9). There are currently no major communities in the project area. The project
would provide new settlers with adequate health, educational, and other facilities
to attain living standards comparable to developed areas elsewhere in Sri Lanka.
The basic project development unit would be a hamlet or unit center of about
250-275 farmer-settler families and 75 non-farmer settlers. There would be about
18 such units in each zone. Two area or block centers would be developed in each
zone, with each block center serving about 8-10 unit centers. One township or
project center would be developed at Meyankolla in Zone 8 to serve the entire
project area.
3.09    About 18,200 new families (approximately 100,000 persons) would settle in
the project area with development, including 14,000 farmer- settlers 1/ and about
4,200 tradesmen, service personnel, and GOSL staff. The project would provide
basic social infrastructure including development and training centers. A summary
of the infrastructure to be provided is given in Annex 1, Table 3. Educational
facil.'ties would include a primary school at each hamlet and junior secondary
schools at block and project centers based on population (including three each in
Zones 6-7 and four in Zone 8). Senior secondary schools would be located at block
centers in Zone 6-7 and at the project center in Zone 8. Limited boarding
facilities would be provided at schools for students coming from more distant
parts of the project area. The project would cover the initial furniture and
1/ Includes about 600 farm-families (4%) from minor tank areas in the RB which
would be incorporated into the project's overall irrigatiorL systen.



-13-
training aids for the schools, and the Ministry of Education would defray recut
rent costs of the buildings, teacher's salaries, and other expenses.
3.10    The project would provide strategically located medical and health
facilities including ambulance dnd mobile unit service. A, central health unit
would be located at Meyankolla, with dispensaries in each zone. Midwife clinics
and sub-dispensary units would be sited throughout the area with three clinics and
dispensary units in Zone 6, four in Zone 7, and six in Zone 8. Each dispensary
unit would serve about four hamlets. Living quarters would be provided for medi-
cal and nursing staff.
3.11    Other facilities would include a townhall and offices, police station and
police posts, telephone and postal facilities, rural electrification, and a piped
water supply for the project center at Meyankolla. Storage for fertilizers, seed,
and food provided by the World Food Programme (WFP) also would be provided at
block centers.
3.12    Management and Operational Infrastructure (Annex 2, Table 9).  Project
settlement and O&M activities would be managed by the Mahaweli Economic Agency
(MEA). Offices and quarters for MEA staff would be provided under the project.
Housing would be included for all MEA management staff at the unit, block and
project centers and dormitories for extension workers and farmers would be
provided at block and project centers. The project would provide office buildings
at block and project centers, a central workshop, and minor repair facilities.
Duriag project implementation, MEA's recurrent costs (e.g., salaries, allowances,
vehicle operation) would be covered as part of project O&M costs.
3.13    Agricultural Training and Research Facilities.  Farmer-settlers, some with
no practical irrigation experience, would require training in water management
techniques, particularly for diversified cropping. They would need to learn the
fundamentals of, and accept responsibility for, proper operation and maintenance
of tertiary irrigation. The key to the success of the Mahaweli Program and its
individual projects is knowledgeable, efficient farmers capable of managing water
resources effectively in cooperation with others. With water use in similar
irrigation projects currently running as much as 2-3 times higher than actually
needed, a major effort must be made to (a) construct proper irrigation systems to
control water issues ;-fficiently and to distribute them equitably, and (b) train
farmers to be good water managers. The Aralaganwila Training Center farm on the
System B LB, which also has soil types typical of the RB, would be used to demon-
strate diversified cropping and irrigation systems. The Center, currently being
developed with CIDA financing, would have necessary classrooms, research build-
ings, laboratories, and farm facilities to demonstrate correct agricultural prac-
tices and irrigation techniques. Hostel accommodations would be provided for [iO
participants attending basic courses. riarters for resident staff, offices,
workshops, and ancillary buildings also would be provided. The center would
organize (a) pre-service and in-service training for management operation staff;
and (b) training for contact farmers and community leaders. It also wo11.cd serve
as a venue for field day demonstrations for the settler community. Small demon-
stration plantations for farm forestry would also be developed.  To complement the
Aralagangwila complex, a small demonstration-cum-training center would be
developed at Meyankolla.
3.14    Fuelwood and Cashews Plantations (Annex 2, Tables 10 & 11).  To provide an
adequate, long-term supply of fuel for settlers, the project would establish



-14-
fuelwood plantations within the project area. These plantations would be located
in upland rion-irrigable areas. Specific locations would be demarcated by the MASL
Planning Division and then released tc the Forest Department (FD) for development.
FD would establish small nurseries and coordinate plantation establishment and
maintenance. Based on an estimated annual famiiy fuelwood requirement of 2.25 m3,
average annual yield of 20 m3/ha, and a six-year rotation, about 1,650 ha would be
required to meet the fuelwood requirements of farnmer and non-farmer settlers. 1/
An additional area of 350 ha would be planted to support industrial development.
About 20% of the 2,000 ha total would be planted each year during project
implementation to ensure a continuous supply. In contrast with past development
practice in Mahaweli projects, homestead plots would not be cleared; rather,
standing timber would be used for fuel until plantation output and homegrown
supplies become available. Another 2,000 ha of non-irrigable land would be demar-
cated by MASI, and developed for cashew cultivation. About 400 ha would be planted
annually and managed by the Sri Lanka Cashew Corporation (SLCC) on behalf of MASL.
Assurances were received from GOSL that by December 31, 1984 SLOC would submit to
the Bank for review and approval a detailed technical work program, including
appropriate costing, for implementing the cashew development component in the
project area. Bank approval of such a program would be a condition of disburse-
ment for this component.
3.15    Conservation Forests (Annex 2, Table 10).   General forest planting would
be carried out in accordance with ongoing conservation programs sponsored by '{ASL
and would be managed by FD. About 1,000 ha in the project area would be
reforested, including riverine areas, roadsides, and canal banks.
3.16    Settlement (Annex 2, Table 9).   Each new farmer-settler would be allotted
on average 1.0 ha of farm land, as well as a 0.2 ha houseplot. The 7.0 ha of
irrigated land would vary from all paddyland to 0.2 ha paddyland wit!- 0.8 ha
upland. Each new settler family would receive orientation and transport to its
assigned area, temporary shelter, basic materials for house construction, farm
tools, and an initial seed/seedling supply. A total of 14,000 farm families are
expected to settle on irrigated land developed by the project, with approximately
4,500 of these in Zone 6; 4,700 in Zone 7; and 4,800 in Zone 8. They would
receive houseplots and farmplots about four to six months before irrigation water
becomes available. Their first harvest would be obtained about one year after
settlement. They would be given opportunity to work for competitive wages in
developing their land, including land preparation, farm ditch and drain construc-
tion, and bunding. Non-farm settlers would construct houses and business estab-
lishments from their own resources, institutional credit, or other programs inde-
pendently of the project. Settlers would be eligible to receive food aid for a
period of 12 months after arrival under agreements between GOSL and WFP. Based on
experience in ongoing Mahaweli settlement operations, no difficulty is foreseen in
attracting sufficient settlers for the project. Provision would also be made for
both individual, livestock holdings and communal grazing areas within the settle-
ment design.
3.17    Settler Selection Criteria.  A scoring system is used to choose settlers
for Mahaweli projects. All applicants must currently work in the agricultural
sector, and cannot be government employees. Applicants receive a score based on
1/ 25% of farmer-settler family fuel requirements would be met from houseplots.



-15-
their family characteristics, and are ranked by this score. There are eight
criteria:  (a) Age:  Maximum score of 10 for the 30-39 group, and lower scores if
older or younger; (b) Education: Maximum score of 10 if completed grade 10 or
above, lower score if less; (c) Tenurial Status: Maximnum score of 10 if landless,
less if a landowner; (d) Personality: Maximum scores of 5 each for physical
fitness and sociability; (e) Farming knowledge of modern methods and formal
agricultural schooling; (f) Size of Household: Maximum score of 10 if 3 units of
labor, lower score if more or less; (g) Participation in Village Activities:
Maximum score of 10 based on degree of participation of applicant and wife in
village level organization; and (h) Other Skills: Maximum score of 10 based on
knowledge o- medicine, masonry, carpentry, and other such skills.
3.18    Agricultural Extension.   A strong agricultural extension program would be
provided to ensure expected yields and intensities are achieved as quickly as
possible. Extension staff would receive thorough training before and after
arrival in the project area. Periodic courses at the Aralaganwila Training Center
would complement proposed pre-service training of field staff. The extension
program would be consistent with the Training and Visit (T&V) System currently
being introduced throughout Sri Lanka. Assurances were received from GOSL that
implementation of agricultural extension services establisaed in the project area
under MASL will be in accordance with the systematic approach agreed to under the
nationwide Agricultural Extension and Adaptive Research Project assisted by IDA.
3.19    Farm Machinery Hire Service (Annex 2, Table 15).   Proposed development of
the project area would be principally for double-cropped paddy. The maha crop
should be planted by end-November to make most effective use of maha rains.
Therefore, farmers must have sufficient farm power to complete land preparation
within a period of about b-8 weeks before the maha crop and 4-6 weeks before the
yala season, as well as assistance to thresh the maha crop. Since necessary farm
power generally would not be totally available irom farmers' own resources durir.g
the early stages of the project, as under Mahaweli III in System C, MEA would
operate a tractor hire service consisting of sixty-four 40-50 HP tractors with
suitable tillage implements provided under the project. It would not be necessary
to replace these MEA tractors because the farming community eventually would
acquire sufficient two-wheel tractors and draft animals to make a publicly
operated tractor hire service no longer neceseary.
3.20    Agricultural Supporting Services.   The marketing and distribution of
seeds, fertilizers, and pesticides would be supported by the construction of a
central store at Meyankolla and smaller stores (two per block and one per unit) to
serve as retail outlets. Since fertilizers would be blended and stored in large
quantities at the Ceylon Fertilizer Corporation regional depot nearby at Badulla,
the project would provide only minimum on-site storage facilities, but would
provide sufficient transport vehicles to replenish stocks on short notice. Sup-
plies over the long-term are expected to come from bulk depots at Polonnaruwa.
3.21    Rural Credit.  Most farmer-settlers withbn the project area would be
selected from the landless, unemployed, and underemployed segments of the popula-
tion and would be dependent upon credit to obtain basic cultivation and domestic
requirements. Furthermore, about 10-20% of the new occupants of System B RB would
belong to the trading and service sectors and would require development capital to
finance investments in support activities. Institutional credit, therefore, would
be an integral part of settlement requirements. Recent efforts to improve credit



-16-
recoveries, including resclieduling (para 2.11), are not expected to affect adver-
sely credit eligibility for those settlers selected under the project. Rather,
settlers are expected to benefit directly from programs complementary to project
investments currently being carried out by major banking institutions to
(a) expand rural lending based on repayment and supervisory discipline,
(b) provide field organizations and facillties, and (c) broaden lending schemes as
new rural financing needs arise. Their lending schemes and scales of finance,
both existing and envisaged, should however be reviewed periodically to ensure
that financial assistance is not eroded by inflation. To permit realization of
agricultural production potential from project investments and to ensure growth of
a viable service sector, assurances were received from GOSL that it will make
arrangements to provide adequate credit to project area residents, that such
institutional credit will be supported by adequate supervisory staff and
facilities, and that credit arrangements will be reviewed periodically and
adjusted as required.
3.22    Monitoring and Evaluation (Annex 2, Table 13).  A monitoring and evalua-
tion program would be carried out under the project to review progress in project
implementation and success in meeting its objectives (para 4.12). In particular
it would focus on two basic areas: (a) input delivery and ase covering civil
works construction, on-farm development, settlement, scheduling, project costs,
O&M, agricultural input supplies, and credit; and (b) output achievement in terms
of increased production, improved services and living standards, employment gener-
ation, water management and use efficiency (including irrigation deliveries and
system losses), and environmental impact. In view of ongoing and comprehensive
assistance from CIDA in MASL's overall monitoring efforts, the project would
include only a series of incremental baseline socioeconomic and agricultural
investigations by which to assess the project's impact.
3.23    Water Quality Monitoring Program (Annex 2, Table 10).   The project wotuld
include development and implementation of a water quality sampling program within
System B. A five-year program would be carried out to collect physical, chemical
and biological data on a regular basis from specific sampling sites in the project
area, i.e., Maduru Oya, irrigation tanks, canal and drainage systems, groundwater
and downstream estuarine areas. Sample analyses would be made in the field and in
a centralized laboratory to be established in the project area. Analyses would
evaluate (a) extent of agro-chemical entry into the aquatic ecosystem as a result
of runoff, (b) cumulative effects of nutrient buildup and other pollutants on
Maduru Oya estuarine resources, and (c) effects of Maduru Oya discharges on the
salinity profile and fisheries resources of the estuary. Based on these monitor-
ing activities, water management problem areas would be identified and corrected,
and proper discharge levels determined to enhance estuarine resources. The
program would be coordinated by a Water Resource Research and Monitoring Unit
established within MASL. Technical assistance would be provided under the project
to assist program design and prepare a mathematical model of salinity characteris-
tics in the estuary.
3.24    Fisheries (Annex 2, Table 12).  At project completion, an estimated 2,000
ha of small natural and manmade waterbodies would exist in thb RB downstream area.
The project would provide the seeding of these waterbodies with fingerlings of
freshwater species (@ 1,000/ha: to stimulate traditional artisanal fishing
activities in the area. The Ir.land Fisheries Development Department, Fisheries
Ministry, would carry out these ::nual seeding operations on behalf of MAST. In



-17-
accordance with its ongoing program. Fingerlings would be obtained from the
CIDA-financed hatchery currently under construction near the Maduru Oya Reservoir.
Water Supply and Demand (Annex 1, Tables 2 to 9)
3.25    Average annual irrigation water requirements were based on cropping pat-
terns anticipated with project development (Annex 2, Chart b). Considering effec-
tive rainfall and an average overall efficiency factor of 65%, the gross annual
irrigation wa.er requirement for the 14,000 ha (net) RB area was estimated to
average about 379 m3, The recently completed Maduru Oya Dam would regulate allo-
cated diversions from the Mahaweli Ganga and inflows to Maduru Oya Reservoir to
serve the following requirements in System B:
Irrigable   Mean Annual
Area (ha)   Demand (M 03)

La  The 3,600 ha Lower LB "drop-off" area is planned for future deve-

3.26    The System B water supply would consist of 380 M m3 of natural inflow to
Maduru Oya Reservoir, 30 M m3 to NDK Reservoir, and an average of 677 M m3 of
Mahaweli Ganga diversions through the link tunnel to Maduru Oya Reservoir from the
Minipe Anicut-Transbasin Canal system to meet the full irrigation requirements of
the LB and Rb areas. The link tunnel has a capacity of 34 m3/sec, which is ade-
quate to convey the supplemental water needed for System B. The capacity of the
Minipe Transbasin Canal is limited to 64 m3/sec and is designed to serve System C
as well as System B. With the regulatory storage provided in the Ulhitiya Oya and
Ratkinda Reservoirs located at the terminus of the Transbasin Canal, adequate
water would be available from the canal to supply the full capacity of the link
tunnel (90 M m3/mo) for all months except during peak demand periods in May-July
when System C demand would reduce the water available for di         0TIon. Maduru Oya
and NDK reservoir storage releases would normally supply che addi:tonal require-
ment of System B during that period. Simulated operation studies for Systems B
and C using estimated reservoir inflows and Transbasin Canal diversions, as
modified by upstream storage and diversions from the Mahaweli Ganga, indicate
estimated irrigation demands for System B can be met without shortage. Neverthe-
less, development of the "drop-off" area in the lower reaches of Left Bank area
would be delayed until the remaining lands in System B and System C are completed
and water availability has been verified. Water-use monitoring and water manage-
ment training programs would be carried out to facilitate effective water manage-
ment and efficient use of irrigation water by farmers. An assurance was received
from GOSL that MASL will allocate to System B tae full amount of water required
for project use.
3.27    With irrigation of the 14,000 ha project area at an average cropping
intensity of 180%, about 20% of the water applied would return to the Maduru Oya
during the month of application and 10% in the second month, amounting to about 35
M m3 of usable return flow in the Yala and 20 M m3 in the Maha. This would more



-18-
than meet the requirement for the presently cultivated land (1600 ha net) in the
downstream Punanai area, to be irrigated from the lower Maduru Oya. The quality
of water in the Mahaweli Ganga and Maduru Oya is satisfactory for irrigation use.
The project water quality monitoring program (para 3.23) would complement the
program already underway with USAID assistance in System B that gathers physical,
chemical and biological data on water supplies and return flows on a regular
basis.
Status of Project Preparation
3.28    Feasibility investigations for System B were completed by Acres Interna-
tional, Ltd. and summarized in its August 1980 Maduru Oya Project feasibility
report. Technical and cost data for the RB area were updated by Acres in 1982, and
the Mahaweli Authority subsequently updated the development plan for social
infrastructure and settlement. A consulting firm was engaged with USAID financing
to prepare final designs and preliminary tender documents for NDK Dam and Feeder
Canal and the Main and Branch Canals. The detailed surveys, geotechnical inves-
tigations and final designs have been completed for both the Dam and Canals. A
contract was awarded for construction of NDK Dam and Feeder Canal (with CIDA
assistance), and construction of NDK Dam and Feeder Canal will be c.-ipleted early
in 1984. Bids for constructing the Main and Branch Canals are expected to be
called in mid-1984 (financed by the Saudi Fund for Development (para 3.29)).
3.29    Land classification for the feasibility report was based on semi-detailed
soil surveys and topographic mapping at a scale of 1:5,000. Detailed irrigation
suitability land classification and drainage investigations were carried out in
late 1983 (following US Bureau of Reclamation criteria) to delineate lands
suitable for paddy and diversified cropping, and the irrigable area was adjusted
accordingly. Designs and estimates for irrigation distribution and drainage
systems were developed from sample areas representative of the project area.
Sample area studies utilized available topographic maos (scale of 1:5,000 and one
meter contours) with land classification ctinerimposed to develop realistic cost
estimates for the distributary canals, field canals, and drains. Final layout and
preparation of detailed designs, cost estimates, and tender documents for dis-
tribution facilities, drains, roads, and social infrastructure would be carried
out with technical assistance provided under the project (para 3.30) utilizing
detailed strip photography.
Technical Assistance (Annex 4; Annex 2, Tables 6, 7, 10 and 11)
3.30    The professional and technical staff of the Mahaweli Engineering and
Construction Agency (MECA) are currently almost fully committed to ongoing
projects in the Mahaweli Ganga Development Program (including System B LB), and
MECA does not have the capacity to provide the engineering and supervision exper-
tise required during project implementation. Consequently, consultants would be
provided to supervise construction of the Main and Branch Canals and to plan,
design, and supervise construction of RB tertiary irrigation facilities, drainage
facilities, and social and management infrastructure. A consulting firm for
supervision of construction of the Main and Branch Canals would be financed by the
Saudi Fund and is expected to be selected and posted in early 1984. The consult-
ants required for final design, layout and construction supervision of tertiary
irrigation and drainage facilities, on-farm development, roads, and social
infrastructure would be finianced by CIDA. Draft terms of reference for the Saudi
Fund and CIDA funded consultants (who would also supervise implementation of

Cost Estimates
3.31    Total project cost is estimated at about Rs 4,351.0 M (US$160.1 M),
including US$32.1 M for price escalation and excluding the Bank front-end fee.
Cost estimates include Rs 35.3 M (US$1.3 M) for taxes and duties on imported
vehicles, equipment a:id materials. Foreign exchange costs would be about
US$85.0 M, or 53%. Cost estimates are based on end-CY83 price levels. Unit
prices for the main and branch canals are based on recent bids for similar work
escalated to end-1983. The cost for NDK Dam Is based on its actual bid price.
Cost estimates for the tertiary irrigation distribution and drainage system are
based on costs of similar facilities being constructed in Systems H and C and
detai'ed layouts for sample areas. Costs of land clearing, on-farm development
and social infrastructure are based on MECA and MEA cost data from other systems,
adjusted to accommodate project conditions. Administration and engineering costs
are based on the construction organization rroposed, which, plus the assistance to
be provided by consultants, amount to about 12% of construction costs. Physical
contingencies were estimated at 10% for the Main and Branch Canals and 15% for
other civil works. Price contingency estimates are based on inflation rates shown
in Table 3.1. Because estimated domestic inflation rates are generally expected
to remain higher than foreign inflation rates, particularly in the early years of
implementation, year-specific exchange rates have been assumed to maintain pur-
chasing power parity. Irrigation O&M costs at full development (1989) are
estimated to average Rs 450/ha (base aosts) or Rs 675/ha including price contin-
gencies. O&M costs for irrigation ard social infrastructure were based on the
operatiotnal organization to be provided and the maintenance schedule to be carried
out annually, including personnel, equipment and materials. Total O&M costs are
estimated at about Rs 30 M annually. The project cost estimates are detailed in
Annex 2 and Tables 3 to 15, and are summarized below:

Financing
3.33    Project cofinancing arrangements are being finalized by GOSL.  The
Saudi Fund for Development has indicated its willingness to provide
US$50.10 M equivalent (about 32% of project cost net of taxes and duties).
CIDA has indicated its intent to contribute about US$40.0 14 equivalent (about
25% of project cost). The above bilateral assistance, combined with the
proposed IDA/Bank contribution of US$42.1 M (IDA credit US$30 M; Bank Loan
US$12.1 M), would finance about 83% of total project costs net of taxes and
duties including 100% of foreign and 64% of local costs, The combined
IBRD//IDA contribution would finance about 26% of the total project cost net
of taxes and duties. The Bank loan would finance in addition the Bank
front-end fee of US$29,925 on a proposed Bank loan of US$12.0 M which would
be capitali2ed into the loan. Remaining requirements (US$28.0 M equivalent)
would be met by GOSL. Satisfactory arrangements for commitments from the
other external project cofinanciers would be a condition of effectiveness for
the Bank Loan/Credit. Assurances were received from GOSL that adequate and
timely funds will be provided for project implementation. Financing of

3.35    Components to be financed by CIDA and Saudi Fund would be procured
in accordance with their respective procedures.  Components involving Bank
financing would be procured by methods acceptable to the Bank. Distribution
and field canals and the drainage facilities (US$11.3 M equivalent) would be
combined into about five packages (US$2 - 3 M equivalent) to attract foreign
competition but would be contracted tlc-ough LCB. These works, because of
their small size and scattered location, are unsuited for ICB; but some
foreign contractors in Sri Lanka may have an interest in such work. There-
fore, on all items procured through local competitive bidding (LCB), foreign
contractors would be eligible to participate. Primary and market roads,



-24-
exclusive of final asphalt surfacing, would be incltuded in one contract
(US$8.6 M equivalent) to be awarded following ICB in accordance with Banki
guidelines. Local firms arc expected to be competitive for the Lrrigation
facilities and road contracts. Upgrading of OEM roads to market road stand-
ards, asphalt surfacing of the primary and market roads following constrxc-
tion of other works (US$3.7 M equivalent), and construction of smaller vL1-
lage roads (US$2.8 M equivalent) would be contracted following ICB procedures
acceptable to the Bank.  Land clearing and on-farm development would be done
by both mechanized and manual methods. Clearing to be done mechanicaLly
would be groupedc into contracts of at least US$1.0 M each to be awarded
following LCB procedures acceptable to the Bank. Work to be done manually
would be divided into snall contracts and awarded in fixed rate contracts to
small local contractors and settlers. Settlement assistance, including
transportation, temporary shelter, housing materials, seeds, seedlings, and
tools would be provided by MEA.  SmalL off-the-shelf items costing less than
US$20,000 per contract that cannot be bulked into packages suiLable for
international tendering or which are required urgently for project implemen-
tation may be procured after solicittation of quotations from at least three
independent suppliers in accordance with procedures satisfactory to the Bank.
Such purchases would be limited to an aggregate of US$0.5 14 equivalent. ALl
work contracts over US$100,000 and equipment contracts over US$50,000 would
be subject to Bank review prior to award. Other contracts would be subject
to selective post-award review. A preference margin of 7.5% D.: civil worlks
contracts and 15% on equipment and vehicles would be extended L. qualified
local manufacturers in bid evaluation.  Taxes and duties on all equipment and
vehicles would be the responsibility of GOSL.
Accounts and Audit
3.36    In view of the difficulties experienced with audit reporting undex
previous Mahaweli Projects and the general lack of adequate record keeping by
MASL, the Bank and GOSL have agreed that all expenditures disbursed on the
basis of statements of expenditure would be audited semi annually instead Af
annually to facilitate disbursement action. MASL has agreed to engage
qualified consultants for establishment and installation of a suitable
accounting system for maintaining records of all Mahaw0li Ganga Develolpnent
Projects. Such assistance would include, inter alia, com?tuter expertise.
GOSL has appointed a local accounting firm to establish a sound accounting
system for all MASL projects and will fund its services oIt of GOSL resour-
ces. Assurances were received from GOSL that MA,SL will: (a) maintain
separate project accounts adequate to reflect the operations, resources, and
expenditures related to the project; (b) have these accounts and financial
statements audited annually by independent auditors acceprable to the Bank
and would submit certilied copies of these audited accounts and auditors'
reports within nine months after the end of each fiscal year; (c) provide
semi-annual audit reports prepared by independent auditors acceptable tx, the
Bank on all expenditures for which reimbursement applications are subnl:ted
without ftull documentation, i.e., on the basis of statements of experiditure ;
(d) ensure that each audit report includes, inter alia, a statement that
funds disbursed against statements of expenditure have been used for the
purposes for whiclh they were provided; and (e) retain up to one year after
credit closing for review by Bank representatives all records (contracts,
orders, invoices, bills, receipts, and other documents) evidencing expendi-
tures claimed for reimbursement by scatements of expenditure.  Semi annual



-25-
audit reports would be submitted within sixty days from the end of each
preceding six-nonth period. Withdrawal applications submitted on this basis
would be certified by the Project Engineer and the Project Accountant. MASL
would submit copies of unaudited annual project accounts to the Bank not
later thaii three months after the end of each fiscal year.;

staff salaries and wages, vehicle O&M, and supplies on a declining basis,
