Environmental Impact
3.39    Natural vegetation in the project area has gradually been diminished
by encroachment over the last decade, with about 10,000 ha currently under
chena rotation. The remaining tropical dry, mixed evergreen forest covers
about 20,000 ha, principally in the western portion of the projact area.
Although site-specific data are limited, a wide variety of wildlife, includ-
ing endemic and endangered/threatened species, likely occurs within tne area.
The Nelugala jungle corridor, a wildlife reserve, extends through both the RB
and LB areas. Based on a 1982 cabinet decision, this corridor will be
declassified when the areas are required for development. An assurance was
received that all portions of the Nelugala Corridor it the project area will
be declassified before the area is developed. There are currently no sub-
stantial fish populations in the Maduru Oya; however, its coastal estuary
provides breeding grounds for shrimp and commercial fish species. MASL plans
to stock Maduru Oya Reservoir with suitable fish species starting in 1984,
outside the project.
3.40    GOSL has taken an active role in environmental protection and conser-
vation. Throughout the planning and irnpletentation stages of AMDP, GOSL has
continued to seek solutions to potentially significant environmental problems
and to integrate them into overall project development. These efforts cul-
minated in a comprehensive environmental assessment of AMDP financed by
USAID. 1/ This study, finalize' in December 1980, presented a detailed
analysis of the effects of AMDP on the terrestrial, aquatic and human
environments, including an evaluation of cumulative impacts of proposed
irrigation.  The final report concluded that AMDP benefits were justifiable
and needed, although achieving them would necessitate certain unavoidable
adverse effects and resource commitments. The report set forth recommenda-
tions to minimize detrimental impacts, enhance beneficial aspects, and
achieve effective wildlife resource management. GOSL used the study's recom-
mendations as a basis for an AMDP Environmental Plan of Action. The Plan was
comapleted in November 1981 and has been adopted by GOSL as the official guide
for implementing environmentally-related activities in AMDP. The Plan com-
prises a series of components, each of which identifies both recently imple-
mented and proposed plans or activities to address specific environmental
issues. The Plan's components of particular relevance to the proposed
project include:
1/ Carried out by the US firm of Tippets, Abbett, McCarthy and Stratton
(TAMS).



-27-
(a)  Forestry Planning and Management.  Forest clearing is closely con-
trolled in AMDP and is limited only to those areas to be cultivated.
The State Timber Corporation is responsible for clearing but is
regulated by a Technical Subcommittee on Forestry within MASL. Fuel-
wood plantations for all new settlements are planned throughout the
ANDP area. FD provides technical inputs and extension services for
seedling plantations. In addition, conservation forests are planned
for specialized areas to control erosion, protect river banks and
improve wildlife habitats. In this regard, MASL has adopted a
general guideline to establish where possible 200 m-wide protection
forests along banks of rivers and streams.
(b)  Wildlife Conservation.  The Plan calls for a series of wildlife
reserves within and ;-.jacent to AMDP areas.  A two-phased plan is
proposed, first focu6ing on establishment of reserve and park bound-
aries and preparation of management plans. During the second phase,
management plans would be implemented to include creation of buffer
zones, habitat enrichment, construction of park infrastructure,
wildlife monitoring, establishment of a wildlife training and conser-
vation center, and overall upgrading of the Department of Wildlife
Conservation (DWLC).  DWLC is responsible for implementing this
program and managing new parks. This program is being funded by
USAID (US$5.0 M) and GOSL (US$1.9 M).
(c)  Water Resources Research and Monitoring.  MASL plans to establish
a Water Resources Research and Monitoring Unit to carry out a water
quality monitoring program and coordinate relevant studies involving
control of aquatic weeds, mangrove conservation, river discharge
monitoring, and monitoring wetland resources.  MASL is supporting
initial research involving wetland ecology, weed infestation, and
preliminary water quality sampling, with equipment provided under the
USAID-financed LB Project.
(d)  Health Care and Sanitation Planning.  A comprehensive health care
program is currently being inplemented with external financing in
System H, System C and System B LB. The national anti-malaria cam-
paign is also being extended to new settlements in the AMDP area.
3.41    Mitigation and Protection Measures for the Project Area.  Principal
negative environmental impacts anticipated with the project would be (a)
reduction of natural vegetation; (b) concomitant loss of wildlife; (c) chan-
ges in water quality and quantity with regard to agro-chemical runoff,
nutrient cycling patterns, and salinity intrusion in the downstream estuary;
and (d) potential spread of communicable water-borne and vector diseases. To
mitigate these, a number of interventions would be included under the project
or are being carried out through complementary programs. First, sufficient
fuelwood plantations (2,000 ha) would be developed to meet settler needs and
conservation forests would be established under the project. Secondly, a
water quality monitoring program currently underway would be strengthened.
rTirdly, adequate health facilities would be provided for settlers as part
of the project and would be enhanced through ongoing UNDP-assisted programs
providing safe wells and latrines in settlements. Fourthly, the Mahaweli
Environment ProjecL is sufficiently comprehensive to minimize and mitigate



-2 8-
adverse impacts on wildlife. In addition, part of the existing Omunugala
Forest Reserve has been recommended for inclusion in the Maduru Oya National
Park as a possible trade-off for the declassification of the Nelugala
Corridor. Finally, project development would be in consonance with the needs
of the people of Sri Lanka by providing settlement oppor'tunities on well
developed agricultural lands with adequate social infrastructure to enhance
the quality of life of settlers.
IV.  ORGANIZATION AND MANAGEMENT
Mahaweli Authority of Sri Lanka (M4ASL)
4.01    MASL, established under the Mahaweli Authority of Sri Lanka Act of
1979, is responsible for implementing the Mahaweli Ganga Development Program
in specially designated areas, including System B. It is empowered to estab-
lish corporations or other operational entities and to take any steps con-
sidered necessary to implement development in such areas. MASL is controlled
by five directors, three appointed by the Minister of Mahaweli Development,
with the Secretaries of the Ministries of Finance and Mahaweli Development
Member Directors ex-officio. One of the appointees is selected to be
Director-General and Chief Executive of MASL. MASL has overall respon-
sibility for coordinating project implementation and its subsequent manage-
ment (Annex 3, Chart 1). Actual execution of such work is carried out
through the two MASL operational agencies described below (paras 4.02 and
4.03).
Mahaweli Engineering and Construction Agency (MECA)
4.02    MECA is responsible for the planning, design, and construction of ;all
project civil works, including irrigation and drainage, roads, buildings, and
public service facilities. MECA organizational divisions include Design and
Construction, Finance and Administration, and Personnel and Training, with
Project Directors for each project (Annex 3, Chart 3). MECA is staffed with
well-qualified personnel but currently its professional and technical staff
are nearly fully committed to ongoing development projects in the Mahaweli
Canga Basin. Consequently, it would be necessary to provide Technical Assis-
tance to MECA for preparing the final plan and designs and for construction
supervision of RB project facilities. MASL would select the cot.sultants
provided for, and MECA would direct and supervise the consultants'
activities.
Mahaweli Economic Agency (MEA)
4.03    MEA is responsible for post-construction implementation of projects,
including settlement, land development and allocation, on-farm development,
water management, agricultural credit, production, marketing and extension,
operation and maintenance of the irrigation system, and development and
management of community and business infrastructure. A member of the board
of directors of MASL is the Chief Executive Officer (Managing Director) of
MEA. MEA's head office is located in Colombo where the Managing Director is
assisted by four Managers (Finance & Administration; Community Services;
Production, Credit, & Marketing; and Investment & Business Development).
They are supported by Directors of Irrigation & Power, Settlement, Planning 6    >
Monitoring, and the Resident Project Managers. A Project Coordinator serves



-29-
as a link between the Resident Project Managers in the field and Directors
and other Managers at tne MEA Head Office, and other Colombo-based (service)
organizations.
4.04    The Resident Project Manager for System B RB would be stationed at
Meyankolla in Zone 8. He would have a Deputy Resident Project Manager (DPM)
to assist him with specialist officers in the functional areas of O&M and
water management, agriculture and lands, community and business development,
and marketing and credit. The project area would be divided into six blocks,
each area serving 2,000-2,500 farm families. Each block area would have 8-10
unit areas, each supporting 250-275 farm families. There would be 54 units
in the project. The Block Manager would also provide technical support to
Unit Managers. The organizational structure proposed for the project is
shown in Annex 3, Charts 3-4.
4.05    Settlement Services.  Supply of agricultural inputs and marketing of
produce from the Project area would be coordinated by the Manager of Produc-
tion, Credit & Marketing. lie would coordinate with existing organizations
such as Paddy Marketing Board, Cooperative Wholesale Establishment,
Multi-Purpose Cooperative Societies and the private sector marketing
entities. He would also assist commercial banking institutions in all
aspects of rural credit, including agricultural production and investment
credit.
4.06    Farm Machinery Hire Service.  MEA would operate a farm machinery hire
service primarily to meet an anticipated farm power gap until farmers acquire
sufficient animal and machine power to cultivate their lands. These services
would be controlled by a Mechanical Engineer in the project O&N section.
4.07  .Plantation and Grazing Lands Establishment and Management   About
3,000 ha of out-of-command unirrigated lands not required for settler
residential facilities would be developed as fuelwood and conservation plan-
tations by MEA and FD. Cashew plantations would also be developed on 2,000
unirrigated ha acquired and cleared by MEA and developed by the Sri Lanka
Cashew Corporation (SLCC). During implementation, MEA and SLCC would explore
the feasibility and modalities of establishing mini-cashew estates of about
15-20 ha each for private entrepreneurs, with technical assistance provided
from SLCC. Communal pasture grounds would be developed around hamlet areas
by MEA for grazing of settler livestock. The DPM (Agricultura) would have
overall responsibility for plantation and grazing land development with
assistance of a Forest Department (FD) officer.
Agricultural Supporting Services

in the project area from rxiaterial provided by the Minor IExport Crops Research

Operation and Maintenance
4.10    A Water Management Panel has been set up under tthe Director General,
MASL to allocate Mahaweli Ganga water for irrigation, power, and other uses
and to apportion irrigation water among the various Mahaweli irrigation
projects. Bulk water issues to each project Would be made by a water manage-

Management Supervisor in each block office would be charged with making water
issues as needed to distribution canals. The Water Management Assistant
(WMA) t the unit level would assist farmers within each turnout area (averag-
ing about 12 farmers each) to distribute water equitably on rotation.  He
would be responsible for communicating water requirements and management
information to farmers and for recommending farmers for participation in
periodic training programs. He would also organize turnout groups and guide
them in maintaining field ditches and drains. The maintenance section under

the DPM wculd be responsible for maintaining the irrigation system down to

that unauthorizeC' water diversions do not occur.

Aralaganwila Irrigation Training Center and Farm
4.13    This Center would be the focal point of project staff and settler

4.14    Agricultural Training Courses
(a) Two seven-day training courses would be organized before each main
crop?ing season for area agricultural staff. Apart from Center
staff, instructors would be SMSs attached to the DPM (Agriculture)
office and participating AOs.
(b) About 300 contact farmers would also receive pre-season train- ing
for about two days. arranged to overlap with training provided for
agricultural staff.
(c)  Field days would be held for farmer groups throughout the growing
seasons to demonstrate particular cultural practices.
4.15    Water Management Training Courses
(a)  In view of the importance of efficient water use to the success of
the Mahaweli Program, water management training would be compulsory
for all MEA staff involved in agriculture or irrigation system opera-
tion. Irrigation engineers and Water Management Supervisors would
attend (i) a basic training course lasting about four weeks, includ-
ing about 10 days of field work, and (ii) periodic refresher ses-
sions. WMAs would undergo two weeks of basic training in water
management practices before posting and would receive additional
training at six-month intervals. Instructors would be senior irriga-
tion staff, outside lecturers, and consultants.
(b) Farmer training in efficient water management practices, ope- rating
rules, and other related fields would be a priority of of MEA and the
Center. The objective would be to have as many area farmers as
possible eventually undergo a two-week training program at the
Irrigation Training Center, during which they would review thoroughly
technical and regulatory aspects of water management and would learn
by using the various irrigation facilities provided at the Training
Center Farm.
V. PRODUCTION, MARKETING AND PRICES,
FARM INCOME, AND COST RECOVERY
Agricultural Development with the Project
5.01    About 14,000 ha in System B RB would receive irrigation water with
the project (Zones 6-8). In addition to this new irrigation capability, the
project would provide a more assured water supply to the older Punanai
settlement-irrigation scheme for about 1,800 ha (net) thereby allowing higher
cropping intensity than possible without project investments. Out-of-command
areas would be developed for fuelwood and cashew plantations and grazing
areas.
5.02    Irrigated Area.  About 88% of the newly irrigated area in Zone 6-8
would be placed under a double-cropped paddy rotation with 100% cropping
intensity expected in the maha and 80% on the yala by full development.
Approximately half of the cropped area under paddy would be transplanted.



-33-
Maha plantings, beginning in mid-October, would be with 3.5-4.0 month
varieties. Yala plantings, completed by mid-April would require 3.0-3.5
month varieties to allow a July harvest. Such a cropping schedule would
ensure optimal utilization of rainfall and, therefore, of supplemental
irrigation water. About 1,700 ha net (12%) upland area would be under
soybeans, vegetables, pulses, citrus tree crops, and coconut. About 1,800 ha
under the Punanai tank scheme would receive more assured water and would
continue growing paddy with the project; average cropping intensity in this
tank area would increase from 100% (currently and without the project) to
180% (Annex 1, Chart 1).
5.03    Homeplots and Plantations.  About 3,200 unirrigated ha in Zones 6-8
would be utilized for settler homestead plots. Homestead plots are expected
to produce a variety of agricultural commodities both for on-farm consumption
and off-farm sales. About 2,000 ha of out-of-command area would be placed
under fuelwood plantations so that by full development all home fuel needs
would be locally produced, either on these plantations or on homeplots.
Another 2,000 ha would be allocated for cashew development.
Future Development without the Project
5.04    At present the project area supports about 2,000 ha of chena cultiva-
tion annually. 1/ In addition, Zones 6-8 contain a number of minor tanks
supplying about 600 ha with supplemental maha irrigation and about 2,000 of
rainfed floodplain maha paddy cultivation. This land-use regime is expected
to remain constant in the future without the project, reflecting current
population sparsity and irrigation constraints likely to continue for the
near-term.
Yields and Production
5.05    Annex 5, Table 1 summarizes present and projected cropped area,
yields, and production. Paddy yields at full development are anticipated to
average 4.6 tons/ha (see para 1.11). With-project yield estimates are based
on the nature of area soils, experience in similar regions of Sri Lanka, and
anticipated success in implementing rigorous water management and improved
agricultural extension under the project. Reflecting assumed continuation of
present chena levels without the project, annual dryland production of about
2,000 tons (in maize terms) 2/ would be foregone. In addition, without the
project, about 12,700 tons of paddy would be produced yearly within the
Punanai system, within minor tank command areas, and on floodplains. In
contrast, with the project, an estimated 116,700 tons of paddy would be
generated annually, implying an increment of 104,000 tons. On upland
irrigated soils, each year about 2,740 tons of cowpeas, soybeans, and
vegetables, as well as 1.9 M coconuts and 2.6 M tons citrus would be
produced. Full development production would be reached in 1993 (paddy and

under cultivation each year.

without-project case.

other annuals) and in 1995/96 (perennials). Total incremental rice, 70,700
tons, the project's principal commodity, would equal about 5% of 1980-82
average production nationwide and 41% of average annual rice imports over the
same perlod. By 1991, project fuelwood plantations would be produci:rg about

would produce 400 tons of fisli each year through the project's waterbody
seeding activities.
Marketing and Pricing of Project Output
5.06    Paddy Marketing and Pricing.  Developmerits since 1978 in the paddy
and rice marketing sector have reflected GOSL's attempt to extricate itself
from the costly obligations of directly guaranteeing food supplies and of
subsidizing retail prices. In January 1978, ration privileges were withdrawn
from about 50% of the population, which led to an abrupt expansion of private
rice marketing. Concomitant with ration program reform was the introduction
of the Food Stamp Scheme (FSS). Under FSS, eligible families (family income
of Rs 300/month or less) receive food stamps that can be used to purchase
rice and other food commodities. Administered prices for these commodities
have since been raised closer co the goal of cost-plus levels. While these
1978-79 reforms at first enabled a move toward a system that truly benefited
needy familie- and compensated them for higher costs of basic food items,
failure to .ate FSS eligibility lists has resulted in the exclusion of some
nec'       -es.  GOSL is currently considering FSS amendments, including
ined~.    nefits and reducing the nuamber of recipients.
5.07    The shift to the private sector has had major implications not only
for the share of harvest passing through public sector agencies, the Paddy
Marketing Board (PMB) and the Food Departmerit, but also for the roles that
each plays. In general, GOSL's policy objective has beerr to expand tthe role
of the private sector while concentrating public sector activities on stabi-
lizing the market within acceptable limits of seasonal price variation.
Critical to the success of such policies is the setting of a range between
floor and ceiling prices to provide adequate scope to the private sector
without adversely affecting producer or consumer interests. GOSL last
increased the Guaranteed Price S_-heme (GPS) paddy price by 9% to
Rs 62.5/bushel (Rs 2,995/ton) during the 1982/83 maha season, about 87% of
the end-CY83 import parity price.
5.08    Rice Imports.  Over the last decade, rice imports averaged 360,000
tons p.a., representing about 29% of consumption. Since 1978, imports have
been decreasing steadily as domestic rice production increased, in part due
to start of Mahaweli scheme production. In 1982, however, poor weather
conditions reduced domestic paddy production to 2.16 M tons--a 3% reduction
from the preceding year; rice imports in 1982 totalled 156,000 tons. A
bumper 1982-83 maha harvest of 1.79 M tons has led the Food Department to
reduce rice import projections for 1983 to 100,000 tons, or approximately 7%
of consumption; failure of the 1983 southwest monsoon (yala) is likely to
make this estimate of needed imports optimistic. Despite considerable suc-
cess in boosting paddy production, population growth, higher per capita
consumption, need to establish buffer stocks, and climatic vagaries are



-35-
expected to keep Sri Lanka a marginal net rice importer for the balance of
the decade (para 1.04).
5.09    Paddy Storage and Milling.  PMB currently has about 335,500 tons of
rited storage capacity and owns 28 rice mills with a capacity of 181,000 tons
p.a.  To supplement its own capacity, PMB maintains a register of about 400
"quota mills," having aggregate capacity of about 600,000 tons, that mill PMB
paddy for administered fees and reimbursement for handling and transport
expenses. In addition, there are approximately 1,500 mills operated by
private traders that buy and sell in the open market and also custom mill for
consumers. In 1982 these mills had approximately 1.2 M tons p.a. capacity--
implying a capability of handling about two-thirds of that year's paddy crop.
Private mills vary in capacity and quality from units comparable with rela-
tively large-scale quota mills to small hullers at village-level able to
process about 200 kg/hr. Past curtailment of capital goods imports led to
significant obsolescence, breakdown, and consequent poor milling quality ini
both the public and private sectors. Private units often lacked cleaners,
separators, and graders. With import liberalization in 1977, increased
availability of machinery and spares has enabled the private sector to assume
the slack created with curtailment of PMB-Food Department activities in
1978-79.  The recently increased role of the private sector has led to a
significant underutilization of PMB milling capacity; in 1982 PMB utilized
only about 10% of its rated capacity. It was expected several years ago that
with further privatization, PMB would lease unused capacity to private
entrepreneurs; to date this has not occurred as many PMB mills were too
large, poorly maintained, or located away fron prime growing areas. PM4B has
recently initiated a program to close down its most obsolescent plants.
5.10    USAID and other donors are financing projects designed to increase
and upgLade rice milling and storage capacity in Sri Lanka. 1/ Because of
ongoing modernization projects, current capacity underutilizations, and
considerable local capital available for such investments, only one small
milling unit (2 tons/hr) and 12,000 tons of transient storage, both financed
by PMB, are proposed for the entire System B area. These facilities will be
located at either Welikanda or Aralaganwila in System B LB.
5.11    Cashew Marketing and Processi.ng.  Over the last decade, GOSL has
placed emphasis on cashew development because of its export potential and the
significant unsatisfied local market. While small-scale cashew cultivation
has existed in Sri Larka for some time, large-scale commercial production
started only in 1968. In 1973, the Sri Lanka Cashew Corporation (SLCC) was
formed to take over large-scale cashew estates begun in 1968, encourage
smallholder cultivation, engage in processing and marketing, and establish
quality controls for exports. SLCC currently operates nine major plantati.ons
with a total of 3,500 ha, ranging in size from 50 to 2,400 ha.  In addition,
SLCC administers a smallholder assistance scheme under which free seedlings
and planting subsidies are provide& to growers. SLCC likewise maintains
1/ The USAID project, begun in 1979, includas .modern milling and storage
facilitires with parboiling capability, provision for moisture meters,
cleaners and driers, standardization of mill capacity arid design, credit
for private millers, technical assistance, and training.



-36-
purchasing centers in principal production areas, relies on private agents
to purchase from private growers, and runs a center near Colombo to process
its own and purchased raw nuts. In addition, a number of private dealers/
exporters engage in cashew trade, including one firm with a 2,000 ton p.a.
facility within the Free Trade Zone. Currently, this facility is coln-
siderably underutilized for lack of raw materials. Relatively labor-
intensive technology is employed for cashew processing in Sri Lanka.
5.12    Estimation of cashew production in Sri Lanka is difficult due to the
dispersed, garden-type nature of a major portion of private cultivation.
Also, production data are somewhat misleading since only about 28Y of SLCC's
plantation area is planted and mature. SLCC, however, has made estimates of
total cashew area at 4,600 ha, about 24% under private control. Cashew
production is roughly estimated at about 1,000 tons of raw nuts (200 tons of
kernels), representing a very minor shato of world production. World market
conditions have be,un to improve after k brief disruption caused by tem-
porary, but relatively significant, sales by India onto the worLd markets in
lieu of established barter arrangements. This was reflected in the 1981 drop
in Sri Lanka's export earnings from cashews by 12% to Rs 64.6 M. The
project's annual production of 1,000 tons of raw nuts (200 tons of kernels)
would allow about a doubling of current production. This incremental quan-
tity, however, should present no marketing difficulty for the longer term
in view of growing local demand, projected growth in international demand
(particularly from higher income countries) and apparent international con-
sumer preference for the Asian cashew on grounds of flavor and quality.
5.13    Other Agricultural Activities.  A variety of traditional cash and
staple crops would be grown on the remaining cropped area under the project.
Incremental output of these commodities can easily be absorbed through farm
family consumption, in local and domestic urban markets, and in export trade
(soybeans, citrus, coconut). With anticipated population growth anrd increase
in real income, there should be an assured market for residual commoditi4s
not exported. Should temporary, localized gluts arise, thereby depressing
farmgate prices, upland farmers would have sufficient crop alternatives to
maintain estimated farm incomes (paras 5.14-5.15). About 0.5 M m3 of fuel-
wood would be produced each year from fuelwood plantations in out-of-command
areas to provide local settlers with basic fuel requirements. Along with
cashew development, the project's fuelwood plantations would be in consonance
with Sri Lanka's agro-forestry program.

Cost Recovery/Impact on Government Budget
5.16    A fundamental objective of the Mahaweli Program is establishment of a
viable and sustainable socio economic environment for a large portion of the
poor within Sri Lankan society.  SettLers are selected for project areas fron
applicants whose current earning potential and agricultural holdings, if any,
are judged inadequate.  They tend to come from areas experiencing overpopuLa-
tion and high levels of unemployment and underemployment. Without the
Mahaweli Scheme, therefore, the bulk of applicants would likely be burdens
to the government budget since they would qualify for food and/or other
assistance payments. With the project, this public subsidy would be reduced,
and saved resources could be reallocated to satisfy other national priorities
(para 5.18).
5.17    Investment and Recurrent Costs.  Investment costs of project
works--NDK Dam and Feeder Canal (prorated), irrigation and drainage
infrastructure, land development, roads, social and administrative
infrastructure, equipment, settlement assistance, monitoring, training,
plantation development, and technical assistance--would amount to about
Rs 2,204 M in present value (PV) terns. 1/ 2/  Irrigation infrastructure and
land development would represent about 69% of this total.  Recurrent expenses
for project works O&M would amount to about Rs 227 M (PV).  Thus, the RV of
investment and recurrent costs over the 25-year time profile studied woLild
total about Rs 2,431 M.
5.18    Reduced Public Assistance.  At a rate of about Rs 120/family/moath,
about Rs 129 n (PV) would be saved in public assistance with the project,
assuming continuation of the food stamp scheme or another comparable pTogram
that would be available to finance other budgetary requirements.
5.19    Sales of Agro-Forestry Products.  Fuelwood and cashew plantations
would be established by the FD (fuelwood) and SLCC (cashews) on behaLf of

administration, but excludes price contingencies.

MASL. At harvest, wood would be sold at public depots to meet local settler
fuel requirements, and cashews would be processed and sold onto domestic and
export markets. Revenues would in turn flow into the State exchequer to
cover budgetary obligations. Wood and cashew sales are projected to generate
Rs 32 K anid Rs 45 M (PV), respectively, in gross revenues.
5.20    Direct Recoveries--General.  Cost recovery in irrigation projects in
Sri Lanka, particularly involving direct water charges, has generally not
been successful to date, despite protracted dialogue between GOSL and the
Bank on the subject. The administration that came to power in 1977 made an
attempt to reactivate earlier laws and promulgate suitable legislation to tax
irrigation beneficiaries anid generate revenues. Recovery of land improvement
costs and costs of O&M of irrigation works was to be carried out under the
Sale of State Lands Law of 1973 and the Land Betterment Law of 1977; under
the latter, land betterment c!larges or water charges could be levied taking
into account amount and dependability of water supplies, agricultural produc-
tion increases, and capital and O&M costs. Unfortunately, neither of these
laws could be implemented effectively as a series of technical and procedural
problems arose which defied resolution; they were therefore dropped in 1978
as a basis for cost recovery. The Government then attempted to reintroduce
provisions for cost recovery through water charges under the Irrigation
Ordinance of 1946, as amended in 1968. It also provided for land ownership
transfer to settlers upon payment of costs of land improvements under the
Land Development Ordinance of 1936, as amended in 1981. These laws have
nationiwide coverage.
5.21    Water Charges.  Under the Irrigation Ordinance, GOSL was empowered
to impose water charges towards recovery of capital and O&M costs of irriga-
tion systems.  In 1978, charges were set at Rs 75/ha in major schemes (com-
prising 80 ha or more) with cropping intensities of over 150% and at Rs 50/ha
for schemes with croppinig intensities of 150% or less. 1/ Water charges were
assessed and some collections made in certain schemes during 1978-80, but
lack of an effective enforcement mechanism and failure of many farmers to pay
led to collapse of the program and cessation of any collections from 1981 to
the present. In early 1981 extensive dialogue was held between the Bank and
GOSL on cost recovery during negotiations for the IDA-financed Mahaweli
Project III in an attempt to introduce Mahaweli Basin-specific water charges
and collections on the grounds that nationwide covenants, including provi-
sions for rate anhancemnents agreed to under previous projects (Mahaweli II
and Tank Irrigation Modernization Projects), were not being follow%d. Under
Mahaweli III, water charges were to be collected in Mahaweli Scheme areas
starting in September 1982 at a level equivalent to 22% of expected O&M costs
and rising to 100% of such costs by 1991. No action was taken in 1982
because of the politically sensitive nature of water charges in the 1982
elections and because of the drought conditions which prevailed during the
earlier 1981-82 inaha season. The Irrigation Ordinance also proved
problematical for implementing a water charge program, partly because of an
unsatisfactory legal basis for enforcement and for lack of funds and a viable
1/  Such rates were estimated in 1981 to cover but a fraction of O&K
costs--about 25% under major tanks and only 15% under the more complex
Mahaweli schemes.



-40-
collection mechanism.  To remedy these deficiencies, Government (a) estab-
lished a Cabinet-levul panel to recommend a reasonable basis for such co! *c-
tions, and (b) throu!,h amendments to the Irrigation Ordinance (i) approved
reintroduction of magistrare court jurisdiction to streamline legal action
against water char!,e payment defaulters, and (ii) empowered 'Zovernment Agents
to repair damaged irrigation works and recover costs from offenders.
5.22    In July 1983, the Cabinet approved the recommendations of the
Cabinet-level panel, including implementation of nationwide annual water
charge collection at the foJ lowing rates: Rs 2,000/ha for major lift irriga-
tion projects, Rs 688/ha for drainage, and Rs 500/ha for gravity irrigation
to cover current average O&M costs. The Cabinet also approved special
advance accounts for 0&M works into which revenues collected would be
credited. To begin the program and phase collections concomitantly with
improved O&M servicing, GOSL has proposed that 50% of such rates be collected
initially (1984) with progressive increase of 20% of the balance per year
until full rates are collected in five years. Thereafter, as permitted under
the Irrigation Ordinance, rates would be reviewed periodically and enhanced
to accommodate any cost escalation. Approval of this program represents a
major step forward, emanating certainly from recognition of past inadequacies
of resource mobilization and increasing O&M requirements for irrigation
works. For the major irrigation schemes under the Mahaweli Program, MASL
proposes to start water charge collection with the harvest of the 1983-84
maha crop (April-June 1984). Advertising of these proposed rates has already
beeii been carried out in all local newspapers and on the radio, and MASL
proposes further publicity and farmer education. MASL has almost eompleted
necessary revisions to specification registers and other documentation, begun
in June 1983, and has finalized designation and instruction of rate collec-
tors to enable water charge collection to begin on schedule. MASL has
promised to communicate with IDA regarding progress in these interim steps
during the months ahead. It has also agreed to provide a collection report
to the Bank once colleci:ions start. Assurances were received from GOSL that
such water charge collection reports will be submitted annually by July of
each year to the Bank and that GOSL will take appropriate actions to inten-
sify collection efforts in case collection performance proves unsatisfactory.
5.23    While GOSL intends in its collection program firstly to set a defined
nominal recovery goal, based on estimated 1983 OEM costs, to be reached by a
specified date (1988) and secondly to phase increases in water charges in
tandem with needed improvements to O&M servicing of irrigation systems,
significant erosion of real value of water charges is likely in future. At
projected inflation rates, the target of Rs 500/ha in 1988/89 would lose
about 38% of its real value, and assuming 0&n costs rise with general infla-
tion, adjustments will be required to close the gap between charges and
average costs and to comply with previously agreed IDA covenants. Toward
this end, it was agreed with GOSL that a mid-term review will be held by July
1986 to assess the success of the water charge collection program, to examine
the degree to which current rates cover annual O&M costs, and to propose a
program for rate enhancements to enable recovery of full annual O&M costs by
or before 1991. About Rs 44 M (PV) in water charges would be recovered from
project beneficiaries with implementation of the current rate regime proposal



-41-
and with enhancement to Rs 500 in real terms by 1991. Until such time as the
recovery of full O&M costs is attained, it was agreed that GOSL annually will
provide the funds necessary for adequate O&M of the irrigation systems.
5.24    Land Transfers.  Land transfers have yet to begin within areas,
although a formalized program of farm boundary demarcation began in System H
in January 1983 as a prerequisite to such transfers. KASL has reemphasized
its desire to implement this transfer program and to recover land improvement
costs from settlers. Under Mahaweli III, for example, GOSL agreed to recover
costs of settlement assistance, and clearing, on-farm development, field
canals, arid field drains. Under the Land Development Ordinance, such trans-
fer payments were to be made over a ten-year payment period at 4% p.a. inter-
est, 1/ preceded by three years of probationary grace. To reduce the impact
of these payments on new settlers, particularly in initial years, and to
accommodate more moderate growth in projected rice prices since Mahaweli III
appraisal estimates, MASL is now considering a payment plan involving
graduated payments. Although such collections within System C area are not
yet due since settlement activities have just begun, MASL proposes in System
H to (a) complete necessary farm surveys by end-1985, (b) finish preparation
of land alienation registers (begun in February 1983), (c) issue permits by
end-June 1984, and (d) begin land payment collection in April 1985. A
similar exercise would be carried out concurrently for System C. Assurances
were received at negotiations that such a program would be implemented by
GOSL. For System B RB, assurances were received from GOSL that it will
implement in the project area of a program satisfactory to the Bank for the
transfer of rights to protect beneficiaries of land improved under the
project in accordance with an installment plan and at rates sufficient to
recover the costs of settlement assistance (housing materials, tools, and
seed/seedling materials), land clearing, on-farm development, field canals,
field drains, and any other improvements agreed to between COSL and the Bank.
It was agree, that GOSL will inform prospective settlers of the proposed land
tmAnsfer price and payment terms before they physically settle on the land.
Based on (a) Rs 27,500 per ha for on-farm works and settlement assistance;
(b) a payment program involving 10 years, 4% p.a. interest, initial payment
of Rs 2,500 and gradually increasing payments; and (c) an interest-free
probationary grace period of three years, land transfer payments would total
Rs 62 M (PV) in real terms.

VI. BENEFITS, JUSTIFICATION, AND RISKS
Overview
6.01      The project would enable development of currently unexploited and
underutilized land resources in the Mahawell. Ganga Basin for intensive
agricultural development. Also, through irrigated agriculture-based settle-
ment, a viable and sustainable socio economic environment would be estab-
lished for many low-incom.- families; the project would create a sound finan-
cial base in irrigated farming for about 14,000 settler families in the
project area, most of whom would be selected from the lower-income strata of
the Sri Lankan population. About 900 existing farm families in the Punanai
Tank scheme would benefit from more assured water supplies. As agricultural



-43-
production expands, secondary and tertiary industries would also develop,
thereby increasing furthler opportunities for economic growth and employment
for another 4,200 non-farming families. Project settlers would be supported
by compreliensive teclhnical and social infrastructure packages to ensure
agricultural success and social well-being. Residue.l out-of-command areas
remaining after homestead developmnent would become grazinig zones and conser-
vation forests as well as fuelwood and cashew plantations, the latter assist-
irg GOSL's agro-forestry and erosion control efforts.  By expanding produc-
tion of import substituting and exportable commodities (rice, soybeans,
cashews, chillies), the project would have a beneficial impact on GOSL's
balance of payments. In total, the project at full development would gener-
ate anniually ani estimated 70,700 tons of incremental rice, about 770 tons of
vegetables, 2,000 tons of pulses, 2,500 tons of citrus, 1.9 M coconuts,
48,000 m3 of fuelwood, and 200 toins of cashew kernels. In addition, about
400 tons of fish would be produced annually in project area waterbodies as a
result of seeding aztivities. Lastly, the project's water monitoring and
forest conservation interventions would be in consonance with GOSL's Environ-
mental Action Program and its goals of development compatible with environ-
mental protection.
Employment and Income Effects
6.02    With Pew agriculture-based settlement and higher land-use inten-
sities, agricultural labor requirements in the project area would expand from
about 0.6 M man-days without the project to about 3.8 M man-days at full
project development. About 0.2 M man-days would represent labor demand in
out-of-command tree crop activities offering off-season employment to settler
families. Farmer-settlers, while expected to satisfy a large portion of
their on-farm labor reqtuirement for crop production, would still hire-in
about half of their labor needs for peak period activities. In addition,
development within System B RB would generate considerable employment in
construction of physical infrastructure, marketing and processing of agricul-
tural produce, and in provision of social services.
6.03    About 94% of direct project beneficiaries would be new settler
families most of whom are currently landless or operating subeconomic hold-
ings. Even with the water and land charges proposed (paras 5.21-5.24), their
average effective income would increase about 2.8 times above without-project
levels. Another 6% of bentificiaries would be farm families currently engaged
in moderately intensive irrigated farming under the Punanai Tank scheme.
These families would earn incomes about 2.4 times without-project levels
through greater cropping intensities and yields permitted by the project.
Direct Foreign Exchange Effects
6.U4    Based on projected initernational prices expressed in end-CY83 con-
stant currency units, the annual gross foreign exchange value of incremental
production of tradeables generated through the project is estimated at about
US$20.0 M, 92% of which would coine from rice production. Adjusting for
foreign exchange costs of traded recurrent inputs (US$5.1 M) and foreign
exchange O&M costs of the irrigation system and settlement and agricultural
support (US$0.1 M), the project's identifiable direct foreign exchange effect
would total about US$14.8 M p.a.



-44-

terms, which when annualized over the 40-year expected project life would
yield about Rs 43 M p.a. (US$1.7 M).  The economy of Sri Lanka would realize
an economic rate of return (ERR) from total project investments, inclusive of
physical contingencies, of about 12%. The following summarizes ERRs and Net

The economic cash flow for the total project is provided in Annex 6, Table 5,
with full referencing of underlying assumptions. The following, however,
outline principal assumptions underlying the economic analysis:
(a)  Project Life and Phasing.  The project would have an anticipated life
of 40 years. Project implementation would require seven years,
incorporating investments in auxiliary structures begun in 1983 with
CIDA assistance. Phasing of project settlement is given in para
3.32. Production on newly settled land is assumed to begin one year
after settlement. Annual crop production would reach with-project
yields/intensities within four years, and perennial tree crops,
within seven years. Fuelwood is assumed raised in a six-year rota-
tion. Full production levels are expect.ed as follows: annual crops,
1993; perennials, 1995/96; fuelwood, 1991; and cashews, 1996.
(b)  Prices.  For internationally traded ;oods, prices were derived from
projected world market prices expreseed in end-CL83 currency values.
Appropriate adjustments were made for freight, handling, processing,
and quality differentials. Local costs and locally valued benefits
were expressed in border rupees through application of appropriate
conversion factors, the principal one being a Standard Conversion
Factor (SOF) of 0.85. To adjust for rural unskilled labor, an
adjustment factor of 0.80 was applied to the non-peak wage, 20% below
the seasonal peak wage. Year-specific prices have been used for
inputs and outputs over the 1985-95 period, after wlhich 1995 prices
were assutmed to remain unchanged in real terms for the remainder of



-45-
the project life. Input and output prices used in the economic
analysis are summarized in Annex 6, Table 1.
(c) Benefits. Incremental crop benefits are based on most probable
yieTds attainable with project investments. Fuelwood produced on
project plantations has been valued on a i.erosene basis.
(d) Investment Costs. The economic analysis of the project included all
costs associated with directly productive investments, i.e., irriga-
tion and agriculture. 1/ The following highlights special treatment
given to major project-related investment costs:
(i,  Maduru pya Dam and Right Bank Transbasin Canal.  These capital
facilities, representing headworks and allied structures, are
completed. As such, they have been treated as sunk costs (base
case). The proposed project would enable raalization of
benefits from the investments already in place. However,
allocations of the costs of these works were made to the project
in a sensitivity test to assess its broader viability wtth sunk
costs (para 6.09).
(ii)  NDK Dam and Feeder Canal.  In addition to supplying irrigation
to the project areas, these structures would also act as a
downstream re-regulating facility (afterbay storage) for
releases from power installations to be constructed at Maduru
Oya Dam. Therefore, 54% of investment costs for these works
have been allocated to the project based on a single purpose
alternative canal alignment without the NDK Dam to meet project
,Area irrigation water requirements.
(e) Crop Production Costs. Per hectare input requirements by crop are
specified in Annex 5, Table 2. The total farm labor contingent,
including family inputs, was assessed according to economic wages
summarized in Annex 6, Table 1.
(f)  Other ProJect Effects.  The following non-quantifiable effects have
been omitted from the economic analysis: (i) environmental impact
(para 3.37); (ii) demonstration effect to farmers outside System B
RB; (iii) remaining extractable timber 2/ and residual wood useable
for fuel; (iv) incremental employment generated from commodity
processing, handling, transport, and marketing; (v) non-agricultural
benefits, e.g., health, well-being, employment mobility derived from
social infrastructure; and (vi) scrap value of project equipment.
1/ Investments in social infrastructure, such as schools, potable water
supply, health facilities, police stations, post offices, temporary
settlement shelter and administrative infrastructure servicing such
socially-oriented investments were excluded from the analysis.
2/ Mosc of the valuable timber within System B RB has already been removed
throughi legal and illicit felling operations.



-46-
Project Risks and Sensitivity Tests
6.06    The Mahaweli Program dominates Sri Lanka's public investment program
and has severely strained financial and real resources. Public capital
spending, of which the Mahaweli Program constituted 35% during 1980-82, has
grown excessively, contributing to unsustainable budgetary and balance of
payments deficits. As a major step to remedy these imbalances, GOSL has
imposed a moratorium during 1983-84 on new projects not already in the
investment program, while projects included in this program but not yet
started will be scrutinized for possible postponement. The proposed project,
scheduled to start in 1985, would be implemented during the time most of the
investment bulge is expected to have eased. Projects designed to strengthen
balance of payments, to complement investments already made, and to receive
substantial donor support are given priority by GOSL in its public investment
program. The proposed project meets these criteria. Nevertheless, an active
dialogue, involving GOSL, the Ban;, and the IMF, continues on the overall
macroeconomic situation.
6.07    Barring any increase in real costs, the project can withstanid up to
about a 1.5-year delay in project benefits and remain economically viable. 1/
Therefore, efficient and expeditious implementation would be required. The
capabilities of MECA and MEA to carry out project works would be strained
because of these and other ongoing commitments, particularly in Systems C and
B. To alleviate this problem, the project includes significant technical
assistance input to ensure completion of work as scheduled.
6.08    Sensitivity Tests.  Sensitivity tests were performed to assess the
impact of variations in incremental cost and benefit streams on the project's
viability. Switching values (SV) for individual and total cost and benefit
streams are provided in Annex 6, Table 6. This table also presents PWs for
total cost and benefit streams with their breakdown into respective
categories. While an OCC of 10% has been assumed throughout the analysis,
SVs were also calculated at 12%. A plot of PWs for total project cost and
benefit streams against various discount rates is presented in Annex 6, Chart
1(A); a locus of all possible SVs for aggregate costs and benefits 2/ is
provided in Chart 1(B). The SVs demonstrate, and the figures illustrate,
that the project can bear only a moderate amount of shock and still retain
economic viability. If total incremental costs rise above 13% or if
incremental benefits fall more than 12%, the project's economic viability
would be in jeopardy. Therefore, economical and expeditious implementation
would be required to avoid real cost increases. The SVs also suggest that
the project would remain economically attractive with up to a 13.5% shortfall
in incremental paddy production, the project's chief crop.
1/ It should be noted that this is a fairly severe test in this project
since it refers to delay in benefits only, holding investment streams
constant.
2/ Discounted at OCC = 10%.



-47-
6.09    When the full cost of NDK Dam and Feeder Canal was allocated to the
project, the NPW fell by 32% to Rs 289 M (ERR = i1%). When one third of
social infrastructure investments were charged against this project ERR
dropped to 11.8% whereas 50% charge resulted in ERR of 11.6%. In another
sensitivity test, the project's incremental costs were augmented by the
allocated sunk costs of the Maduru Oya Dam and Link Tunnel as well as the RB
Transbasin Canal, System B's principal headworks installations. Begun in
1980, these structures were completed in 1983 and 1982, respectively. Alloca-
tions of these sunk costs were made on the basis of percent of total area to
benefit: accordingly, 41% of Maduru Oya Dam and Link Tunnel (Rs 859 M (PW))
and 25% of the Right Bank Transbasin Canal costs (Rs 187 X (PW)) were
included in the sensitivity analysis. As a result, the project's ERR dropped
from 12% (base case) to 7%. 1/ This outcome confirms the conclusions that
can be drawn from the 1979 Nedeco Implementation Stragegy Study that System B
RB should have had low priority, on economic grounds, among the downstream
development areas. However, given the current sunk nature of these costs,
the project is economically justifiable.
VII. RECONMENDATIONS
7.01    The following assurances were obtained from GOSL:
(a)  the Sri Lanka Cashew Corporation by December 31, 1984 will submit
to the Bank for its review and approval a detailed technical work
program, with appropriate costing, for implementing the cashew
development component in the project area (para 3.14);
(b)  implementation of agricultural extension services established in the
project area will be in accordance with the systematic approach under
the nationwide Agricultural Extension and Adaptive Research Project
assisted by IDA (para 3.18);
(c)  with regard to rural credit:
(i) GOSL will make arrangements to provide adequate rural credit
to project-area residents;
(ii)  such institutional credit will be supported by adequate super-
visory staff and facilities; and
(iii)  credit arrangements will be reviewed periodically and adjusted
as required (para 3.21);
(d)  GOSL will allocate to System B the full amount of water required from
the Mahaweli Ganga for project use (para 3.26);
(e)  adequate and timely funds will be provided for project implementation
(para 3.33);
(f)  with regards to accounts and audits, MASL:
1/  6.5% with full NDK and Feeder Canal costs.



-48-
(1)  will maintain separate project accounts adequate to reflect the
operations, resources, and expenditures related to the project;
(ii) have these accounts and financial statements audited annually
by independent auditors acceptable to the Bank;
(iii)  ensure that each auditors' report includes, inter alia, a state-
ment that funds disbursed against statements of expenditure have
been used for the purposes for which they were provided; and
(iv) retain up to one year after credit closing for review by Bank
representatives alL records (contracts, orders, invoices, bills,
receipts, and other documents) as evidence of expenditures
claimed for reimbursement by statements of expenditure (para
3.36);
(g)  with regard to the environment, all portions of Nelugala Corridor in
the project area will be declassified before the area is developed
(para 3.39);
(h) with regard to project monitoring, MASL will implement a monitoring
and evaluation program for the project in consonance with ongoing
monitoring and evaluation activities in other Mahaweli projects (para
4.12);
(i) with regard to water charges:
fi) GOSL will submit detailed reports by July of each year to the
Bank giving collection progress nationwide and in specific
Bank-assisted irrigation project areas (para 5.22);
(ii) it will take appropriate actions to intensify collection efforts
in case collection performance proves unsatisfactory (para 5.22);
and
(iii) a mid-term review will be held by July 31, 1986 to assess the
success of the water charge collection program, to examine the
degree to which current rates cover annual O&M costs, and to
propose a program for rate enhancements to enable recovery or
full annual O&M costs by or before 1991 (para 5.23); and
(iv)  Until such time as the recov'ry of full O&E  costs is attained,
GOSL annually will provide funds necessary for adequate operation
and maintenance of the irrigation system (para 5.23);
(j) a land transfer program will be implemented by GOSL (para 5.24); and
(k) with regard to such land transfers:
(i) GOSL will implement in the project area a program satis- factory
to the Bank for the transfer of rights to project beneficiaries
of land improved under the project in accordance with an
installment plan and at rates sufficient to recover the costB of



-49-
settlement assistance (housing materials, tools, and
seed/seedling materials), land clearing, on-farm development,
field canals, field drains, and any other improvements agreed to
between GOSL and the Bank (para 5.24); and
(ii)  GOSL will inform prospective settlers of the proposed land
transfer price and payment terms before they physically settle
on the land (para 5.24).
7.02    Conditions of effectiveness of the proposed IDA credit and Bank loan
would be that (i) satisfactory arrangements for commitments from other exter-
nal project co-financiers have been made (para 3.33) and (ii) a Project
Special Account has been opened by GOSL to facilitate project implementatior.
(para 3.38).
7.03    A condition of disbursement against expenditures for the cashew
developaent component of the project would be prior Bank approval of a
detailed technical work program for implementing the component (para 3.14).
7.04    With the above agreements and conditions, the propostl project would
be suitable for IDA/IBRD assistance of US$42.1 M, representing a blend of IDA
credit of SDR 28.3 M (US$30.0 M equivalent) and Bank loan of US$12.1 M. The
Borrower would be the Democratic Socialist Republic of Sri Lanka.

ANNEX 4
-82--                   Page 1
Sri Lanka
Mahaweli Ganga Development Project IV
Draft Terms of Reference for Consulting Services
Tertiary and Drainage System, Roads,
On-Farm Development and Social Infrastructure
Consulting Services for Implementation Planning and Scheduling, Contract
Administration, and Construction Supervision for the Tertiary Irrigation System
and Social Infrastructure.
A. Duty Station
Project Manager's Office for Zones 6, 7 and 8 of System B,
Mahaweli Ganga Development Project IV, Sri Lanka.
B. Date Required
January 1, 1984
C. Duration
Four to five years. The duraton of each technical discipline
would be approximately as shown in attached list.
D. Service Required
The objective of the const.itancy services would be to provide
modern, efficient irrigation aud drainage facilities from the Main
and Branch canals to the individual farms, to prepare plans for
roads, settlement and social infra- structure, and provide water
management advice and assistance. The scope of consultant services
would include the following:
1. Conduct surveys and prepare overall plans, layouts and designs for the
farm plots, tertiary and field irrigation system, drainage system, road networks,
settlement facilities and administrative centers.
2. Prepare final designs and tender documents for both international and
local contract bidding for the tertiary irrigation and drainage facilities and
roads, assist in the prequalification of tenderers and evaluation of tenders, and
recommend to MECA award of contracts to thc lowest responsive, competent firms or
bidders.



ANNEX 4
-83-                          Page 2
3. Plan, coordinate and schedule all work activity, including tender actions
and contract work. Prepare critical work program charts and schedules for deter-
mining critical work actions necessary to implement work within agreed time frame.
Make continuous review of contractor's activities and recommend changes in program
as needed. Keep records of work progress for monthly contractor work payments.
4. Review activity of contractor work forces in the field and provide inspec-
tic necessary to obtain work of good quality and to insure adherence to provi-
sions of the contract specifications.
5. Investigate cropping pattern suitability for project lands in zcnes 6, 7
and 8 of System B, determine crop water requirements, recommend and assist in
achieving efficient irrigation methods and practices, and prepare program for
operation and maintenance of the irrigation system.
6. Provide guidance to on-farm development staff, small contractors and
settlement groups in the location and construction of farm ditches and drains and
soil conservation bunds. Determine most appropriate farm layouts, for efficent
water use for both upland and paddy crops, including grading, leveling and paddy
bunding requirements.
7. The consultant shall prepare and submit the following reports to MASL in
English:
(a)   Monthly Progress Report - at the end of each month, containing
details of personnel mobilization, monthly progress on work,
problems encountered, and anticipated services for the next
month.
(b)   Design Report - to be submitted prior to the tender opening
date, containing hydraulic and structural calculations, work
quantities, cost estimates, construction schedules and detailed
design drawings.
(c)   Tender Documents - including form of tender, specifications and
tender drawings to be pepared prior to issuance of the
documents to pre-qualified tenderers.
(d)   Evaluation Reports - to be prepared immediately after evaluation
of each of pre-qualification of tenderers and tender documents
submitted by pre-qualified tenderers.
(e)   Manual of Operation and Maintenance of irrigation facilities
and water management.
(f)   Completion Report - including "as-built" drawings.



ANNEX 4
Page 3
-84-
E.  Expertise Reguired
The following partial list of expertise shall be required for the
Consultants' professional services.
(i) Team Leader, Senior Engineer - with minimum experience of 15 years
in designing and supervision of construction on canal distribution systems,
related structures and on-farm development and management works, He shall have
substantial experience in managing and implementing irrigation development.
(ii) Physical Planning Engineer - with minimum experience of 15 years in
investigation, evaluation and overall layout of irrigation distribution and
drainage schemes. He shall have substantial experience in farm plot layout,
on-farm development, water delivery and farm turnout arrangements, facility layout
and route selection, access and market road requirements, and the requirement,
location and accessablity of community facilities, services and utilities.
(iii) irrigation Design Engineer - should have a minimum of 10 years
experience in the design and implementation of tertiary distribution and drainage
facilities including irrigation water requirements, hydraulic flow characteristics
in open and closed conduits, water conveyaince losses and irrigation efficiencies,
and characteristics and economics of various methods of canal lining.
(iv) Agronomist - shall be a Graduate with Post-Graduate qualifications
in Agronomy with 15 years Post-graduate experience, 5 of which should be in plant
varieties adaptable to the project area. He should be qualified to plan and
recommend on crop varietal selections and cropping patterns to maximize production
in small farms under tropical conditions, and provide guidance in crop cultivation
practices, adaptive research, and monitoring of inputs and crop production.  He
shall recommend organizational plans and operation of agricultural extension
service for the project and assure uniformity of the extension approach with the
ongoing nationiwide program.
tv) Water Management Specialist - should have sufficient experience of
12 to 15 years in field water management practices and operation and maintenance
of irrigation sy - s. He should be qualified to develop a sound and efficient
water management program. He would work wit", the Training Center staff in
developing training programs for technical staff and farmers to develop effective
water management knowledge and skills. He would also prepare a comprehensive
manual for Operation and Maintenance of the project irrigation facilities.
(vi) On-farm Development Specialist - should be a Senior Agricultural
Engineer with a minimum of 10 years experience in land clearing and development of
virgin landr, caechanical l.nd preparation of cultivated paddy and upland crops
land, farm plot layout, leveling and bunding, and landscaping and soil conserva-
tion work.

-85-                                 Page 4
(vii) Structure Design Engineer - must have a degree in structural
engineering with a minimum of 10 years experience in the design and layout of
road systems, bridges, culverts and foundation treatment. Should also be
qualified in the design of village and community streets and utilities.
(viii) Land Classification and Drainage Specialist - should have a
graduate degree, or equivalent, in the soil sciences with at least 10 years
experience in the classification and evaluation of agricultural soils and lands
for suitability for productin;i of crops in tropical and semi-tropical environment.
Must have experience in the use of areal photographs, topographic maps, and
isometric plottings of soil depths and strata, groundwater tables, and percolation
barriers and their use in preparing accurate land classification maps and measure-
ments. Must also have experience and working expertise in land drainage require-
ments and design. Must be qualified to train local land classification personnel
:taff.
(ix) Settl_ment Planning Engineer - should be a graduate in civil or
architectual engineering with sufficient experience of about 12 years in
integrated community planning and develo-pment, foundation evaluation and treat-
ment, building structural design, and landscape architecture. Should also be
qualified in statistical evaluation of population space and movement, service
utility, and produce marketing requirements.
*,x) Contract Administration Engineer - with qualifying experience of 10
to 15 years in civil construction contract administration, preparation of tender
documents, and review and evaluation of tenders. Should also have sufficient
experience in the evaluation and recording of construction progress, quantity
measurements, extra work orders and claims, contract payments, and the maintenance
of adequate official records of construction quantities and payments, and estab-
lishment of a workable accounting system.
(xi) Surveyor - with at least 15 years experience in internationally
accented accurate and precise methods of topographic, alignment, and construction
material quantity surveys, utilizing modern equipment and technology. Should be
qualified to train and direct local survey staff personnel in these accurate
survey methods.
(xii) Construction Inspector - with sufficient experience of about 20
years in internationally acceptable methods of construction inspection, testing
and recording of quantities. Must be qualified to train construction inspectors
a'signed from local staff in these inspection and testing methods and laboratory
analysis of eoncrete samples, soils and other construction materials.
